Her mother did not interfere with her; but her\r\nfather used to be very indignant with his daughter, for her--as he\r\ncalled it--vulgar soft-heartedness, and declared there was not room to\r\nmove for the cats and dogs in the house. \xe2\x80\x98Lenotchka,\xe2\x80\x99 he would shout to\r\nher, \xe2\x80\x98come quickly, here\xe2\x80\x99s a spider eating a fly; come and save the poor\r\nwretch!\xe2\x80\x99 And Lenotchka, all excitement, would run up, set the fly free,\r\nand disentangle its legs. \xe2\x80\x98Well, now let it bite you a little, since you\r\nare so kind,\xe2\x80\x99 her father would say ironically; but she did not hear him.\r\nAt ten years old Elena made friends with a little beggar-girl, Katya,\r\nand used to go secretly to meet her in the garden, took her nice things\r\nto eat, and presented her with handkerchiefs and pennies; playthings\r\nKatya would not take. She would sit beside her on the dry earth among\r\nthe bushes behind a thick growth of nettles; with a feeling of delicious\r\nhumility she ate her stale bread and listened to her stories. Katya had\r\nan aunt, an ill-natured old woman, who often beat her; Katya hated her,\r\nand was always talking of how she would run away from her aunt and live\r\nin \xe2\x80\x98_God\xe2\x80\x99s full freedom_\xe2\x80\x99; with secret respect and awe Elena drank in\r\nthese new unknown words, stared intently at Katya and everything about\r\nher--her quick black, almost animal eyes, her sun-burnt hands, her\r\nhoarse voice, even her ragged clothes--seemed to Elena at such times\r\nsomething particular and distinguished, almost holy. Elena went back\r\nhome, and for long after dreamed of beggars and God\xe2\x80\x99s freedom; she would\r\ndream over plans of how she would cut herself a hazel stick, and put on\r\na wallet and run away with Katya; how she would wander about the roads\r\nin a wreath of corn-flowers; she had seen Katya one day in just such a\r\nwreath. If, at such times, any one of her family came into the room, she\r\nwould shun them and look shy. One day she ran out in the rain to meet\r\nKatya, and made her frock muddy; her father saw her, and called her a\r\nslut and a peasant-wench. She grew hot all over, and there was something\r\nof terror and rapture in her heart. Katya often sang some half-brutal\r\nsoldier\xe2\x80\x99s song. Elena learnt this song from her.... Anna Vassilyevna\r\noverheard her singing it, and was very indignant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where did you pick up such horrors?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked her daughter.\r\n\r\nElena only looked at her mother, and would not say a word; she felt that\r\nshe would let them tear her to pieces sooner than betray her secret, and\r\nagain there was a terror and sweetness in her heart. Her friendship with\r\nKatya, however, did not last long; the poor little girl fell sick of\r\nfever, and in a few days she was dead.\r\n\r\nElena was greatly distressed, and spent sleepless nights for long after\r\nshe heard of Katya\xe2\x80\x99s death. The last words of the little beggar-girl\r\nwere constantly ringing in her ears, and she fancied that she was being\r\ncalled....\r\n\r\nThe years passed and passed; swiftly and noiselessly, like waters\r\nrunning under the snow, Elena\xe2\x80\x99s youth glided by, outwardly uneventful,\r\ninwardly in conflict and emotion. She had no friend; she did not get\r\non with any one of all the girls who visited the Stahovs\xe2\x80\x99 house. Her\r\nparents\xe2\x80\x99 authority had never weighed heavily on Elena, and from her\r\nsixteenth year she became absolutely independent; she began to live a\r\nlife of her own, but it was a life of solitude. Her soul glowed, and the\r\nfire died away again in solitude; she struggled like a bird in a cage,\r\nand cage there was none; no one oppressed her, no one restrained her,\r\nwhile she was torn, and fretted within. Sometimes she did not understand\r\nherself, was even frightened of herself. Everything that surrounded her\r\nseemed to her half-senseless, half-incomprehensible. \xe2\x80\x98How live without\r\nlove? and there\xe2\x80\x99s no one to love!\xe2\x80\x99 she thought; and she felt terror\r\nagain at these thoughts, these sensations. At eighteen, she nearly\r\ndied of malignant fever; her whole constitution--naturally healthy\r\nand vigorous--was seriously affected, and it was long before it could\r\nperfectly recover; the last traces of the illness disappeared at last,\r\nbut Elena Nikolaevna\xe2\x80\x99s father was never tired of talking with some\r\nspitefulness of her \xe2\x80\x98nerves.\xe2\x80\x99 Sometimes she fancied that she wanted\r\nsomething which no one wanted, of which no one in all Russia dreamed.\r\nThen she would grow calmer, and even laugh at herself, and pass day\r\nafter day unconcernedly; but suddenly some over-mastering, nameless\r\nforce would surge up within her, and seem to clamour for an outlet. The\r\nstorm passed over, and the wings of her soul drooped without flight; but\r\nthese tempests of feeling cost her much. However she might strive not\r\nto betray what was passing within her, the suffering of the tormented\r\nspirit was expressed in her even external tranquillity, and her parents\r\nwere often justified in shrugging their shoulders in astonishment, and\r\nfailing to understand her \xe2\x80\x98queer ways.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nOn the day with which our story began, Elena did not leave the window\r\ntill later than usual. She thought much of Bersenyev, and of her\r\nconversation with him. She liked him; she believed in the warmth of his\r\nfeelings, and the purity of his aims. He had never before talked to her\r\nas on that evening. She recalled the expression of his timid eyes, his\r\nsmiles--and she smiled herself and fell to musing, but not of him. She\r\nbegan to look out into the night from the open window. For a long time\r\nshe gazed at the dark, low-hanging sky; then she got up, flung back her\r\nhair from her face with a shake of her head, and, herself not knowing\r\nwhy, she stretched out to it--to that sky--her bare chilled arms; then\r\nshe dropped them, fell on her knees beside her bed, pressed her face\r\ninto the pillow, and, in spite of all her efforts not to yield to the\r\npassion overwhelming her, she burst into strange, uncomprehending,\r\nburning tears.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next day at twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Bersenyev set off in a return coach\r\nto Moscow. He had to get some money from the post-office, to buy some\r\nbooks, and he wanted to seize the opportunity to see Insarov and have\r\nsome conversation with him. The idea had occurred to Bersenyev, in the\r\ncourse of his last conversation with Shubin, to invite Insarov to stay\r\nwith him at his country lodgings. But it was some time before he found\r\nhim out; from his former lodging he had moved to another, which it was\r\nnot easy to discover; it was in the court at the back of a squalid stone\r\nhouse, built in the Petersburg style, between Arbaty Road and Povarsky\r\nStreet. In vain Bersenyev wandered from one dirty staircase to another,\r\nin vain he called first to a doorkeeper, then to a passer-by. Porters\r\neven in Petersburg try to avoid the eyes of visitors, and in Moscow much\r\nmore so; no one answered Bersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s call; only an inquisitive tailor,\r\nin his shirt sleeves, with a skein of grey thread on his shoulder,\r\nthrust out from a high casement window a dirty, dull, unshorn face, with\r\na blackened eye; and a black and hornless goat, clambering up on to a\r\ndung heap, turned round, bleated plaintively, and went on chewing the\r\ncud faster than before. A woman in an old cloak, and shoes trodden\r\ndown at heel, took pity at last on Bersenyev and pointed out Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlodging to him. Bersenyev found him at home. He had taken a room with\r\nthe very tailor who had stared down so indifferently at the perplexity\r\nof a wandering stranger; a large, almost empty room, with dark green\r\nwalls, three square windows, a tiny bedstead in one corner, a little\r\nleather sofa in another, and a huge cage hung up to the very ceiling;\r\nin this cage there had once lived a nightingale. Insarov came to meet\r\nBersenyev directly he crossed the threshold, but he did not exclaim,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, it\xe2\x80\x99s you!\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98Good Heavens, what happy chance has brought you?\xe2\x80\x99 He\r\ndid not even say, \xe2\x80\x98How do you do?\xe2\x80\x99 but simply pressed his hand and led\r\nhim up to the solitary chair in the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sit down,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, and he seated himself on the edge of the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am, as you see, still in disorder,\xe2\x80\x99 added Insarov, pointing to a pile\r\nof papers and books on the floor, \xe2\x80\x98I haven\xe2\x80\x99t got settled in as I ought.\r\nI have not had time yet.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov spoke Russian perfectly correctly, pronouncing every word fully\r\nand purely; but his guttural though pleasant voice sounded somehow not\r\nRussian. Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s foreign extraction (he was a Bulgarian by birth)\r\nwas still more clearly marked in his appearance; he was a young man\r\nof five-and-twenty, spare and sinewy, with a hollow chest and knotted\r\nfingers; he had sharp features, a hooked nose, blue-black hair, a low\r\nforehead, small, intent-looking, deep-set eyes, and bushy eyebrows; when\r\nhe smiled, splendid white teeth gleamed for an instant between his thin,\r\nhard, over-defined lips. He was in a rather old but tidy coat, buttoned\r\nup to the throat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why did you leave your old lodging?\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev asked him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This is cheaper, and nearer to the university.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But now it\xe2\x80\x99s vacation.... And what could induce you to stay in the\r\ntown in summer! You should have taken a country cottage if you were\r\ndetermined to move.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov made no reply to this remark, and offered Bersenyev a pipe,\r\nadding: \xe2\x80\x98Excuse me, I have no cigarettes or cigars.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev began smoking the pipe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here have I,\xe2\x80\x99 he went on, \xe2\x80\x98taken a little house near Kuntsovo, very\r\ncheap and very roomy. In fact there is a room to spare upstairs.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov again made no answer.\r\n\r\nBersenyev drew at the pipe: \xe2\x80\x98I have even been thinking,\xe2\x80\x99 he began\r\nagain, blowing out the smoke in a thin cloud, \xe2\x80\x98that if any one could\r\nbe found--you, for instance, I thought of--who would care, who would\r\nconsent to establish himself there upstairs, how nice it would be! What\r\ndo you think, Dmitri Nikanorovitch?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov turned his little eyes on him. \xe2\x80\x98You propose my staying in your\r\ncountry house?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes; I have a room to spare there upstairs.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thanks very much, Andrei Petrovitch; but I expect my means would not\r\nallow of it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My means would not allow of my living in a country house. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimpossible for me to keep two lodgings.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But of course I\xe2\x80\x99--Bersenyev was beginning, but he stopped short. \xe2\x80\x98You\r\nwould have no extra expense in that way,\xe2\x80\x99 he went on. \xe2\x80\x98Your lodging here\r\nwould remain for you, let us suppose; but then everything there is very\r\ncheap; we could even arrange so as to dine, for instance, together.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov said nothing. Bersenyev began to feel awkward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You might at least pay me a visit sometime,\xe2\x80\x99 he began, after a short\r\npause. \xe2\x80\x98A few steps from me there\xe2\x80\x99s a family living with whom I want\r\nvery much to make you acquainted. If only you knew, Insarov, what a\r\nmarvellous girl there is there! There is an intimate friend of mine\r\nstaying there too, a man of great talent; I am sure you would get on\r\nwith him. [The Russian loves to be hospitable--of his friends if he can\r\noffer nothing else.] Really, you must come. And what would be better\r\nstill, come and stay with me, do. We could work and read together....\r\nI am busy, as you know, with history and philosophy. All that would\r\ninterest you. I have a lot of books.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov got up and walked about the room. \xe2\x80\x98Let me know,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98how\r\nmuch do you pay for your cottage?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A hundred silver roubles.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And how many rooms are there?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Five.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then one may reckon that one room costs twenty roubles?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, one may reckon so.... But really it\xe2\x80\x99s utterly unnecessary for me.\r\nIt simply stands empty.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps so; but listen,\xe2\x80\x99 added Insarov, with a decided, but at the same\r\ntime good-natured movement of his head: \xe2\x80\x98I can only take advantage of\r\nyour offer if you agree to take the sum we have reckoned. Twenty roubles\r\nI am able to give, the more easily, since, as you say, I shall be\r\neconomising there in other things.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course; but really I am ashamed to take it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Otherwise it\xe2\x80\x99s impossible, Andrei Petrovitch.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, as you like; but what an obstinate fellow you are!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov again made no reply.\r\n\r\nThe young men made arrangements as to the day on which Insarov was to\r\nmove. They called the landlord; at first he sent his daughter, a little\r\ngirl of seven, with a large striped kerchief on her head; she listened\r\nattentively, almost with awe, to all Insarov said to her, and went away\r\nwithout speaking; after her, her mother, a woman far gone with child,\r\nmade her appearance, also wearing a kerchief on her head, but a very\r\ndiminutive one. Insarov informed her that he was going to stay at a\r\ncottage near Kuntsovo, but should keep on his lodging and leave all his\r\nthings in their keeping; the tailor\xe2\x80\x99s wife too seemed scared and\r\nwent away. At last the man himself came in: he seemed to understand\r\neverything from the first, and only said gloomily: \xe2\x80\x98Near Kuntsovo?\xe2\x80\x99 then\r\nall at once he opened the door and shouted: \xe2\x80\x98Are you going to keep the\r\nlodgings then?\xe2\x80\x99 Insarov reassured him. \xe2\x80\x98Well, one must know,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated\r\nthe tailor morosely, as he disappeared.\r\n\r\nBersenyev returned home, well content with the success of his proposal.\r\nInsarov escorted him to the door with cordial good manners, not common\r\nin Russia; and, when he was left alone, carefully took off his coat, and\r\nset to work upon sorting his papers.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the evening of the same day, Anna Vassilyevna was sitting in her\r\ndrawing-room and was on the verge of weeping. There were also in the\r\nroom her husband and a certain Uvar Ivanovitch Stahov, a distant cousin\r\nof Nikolai Artemyevitch, a retired cornet of sixty years old, a man\r\ncorpulent to the point of immobility, with sleepy yellowish eyes, and\r\ncolourless thick lips in a puffy yellow face. Ever since he had retired,\r\nhe had lived in Moscow on the interest of a small capital left him by\r\na wife who came of a shopkeeper\xe2\x80\x99s family. He did nothing, and it is\r\ndoubtful whether he thought of anything; if he did think, he kept his\r\nthoughts to himself. Once only in his life he had been thrown into a\r\nstate of excitement and shown signs of animation, and that was when he\r\nread in the newspapers of a new instrument at the Universal Exhibition\r\nin London, the \xe2\x80\x98contro-bombardon,\xe2\x80\x99 and became very anxious to order this\r\ninstrument for himself, and even made inquiries as to where to send\r\nthe money and through what office. Uvar Ivanovitch wore a loose\r\nsnuff-coloured coat and a white neckcloth, used to eat often and much,\r\nand in moments of great perplexity, that is to say when it happened to\r\nhim to express some opinion, he would flourish the fingers of his right\r\nhand meditatively in the air, with a convulsive spasm from the first\r\nfinger to the little finger, and back from the little finger to the\r\nfirst finger, while he articulated with effort, \xe2\x80\x98to be sure... there\r\nought to... in some sort of a way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nUvar Ivanovitch was sitting in an easy chair by the window, breathing\r\nheavily; Nikolai Artemyevitch was pacing with long strides up and\r\ndown the room, his hands thrust into his pockets; his face expressed\r\ndissatisfaction.\r\n\r\nHe stood still at last and shook his head. \xe2\x80\x98Yes;\xe2\x80\x99 he began, \xe2\x80\x98in our\r\nday young men were brought up differently. Young men did not permit\r\nthemselves to be lacking in respect to their elders. And nowadays, I can\r\nonly look on and wonder. Possibly, I am all wrong, and they are quite\r\nright; possibly. But still I have my own views of things; I was not born\r\na fool. What do you think about it, Uvar Ivanovitch?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nUvar Ivanovitch could only look at him and work his fingers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena Nikolaevna, for instance,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Nikolai Artemyevitch, \xe2\x80\x98Elena\r\nNikolaevna I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to understand. I am not elevated enough for\r\nher. Her heart is so large that it embraces all nature down to the least\r\nspider or frog, everything in fact except her own father. Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall very well; I know it, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble myself about it. For\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s nerves and education and lofty aspirations, and all that is not\r\nin my line. But Mr. Shubin... admitting he\xe2\x80\x99s a wonderful artist--quite\r\nexceptional--that, I don\xe2\x80\x99t dispute; to show want of respect to his\r\nelder, a man to whom, at any rate, one may say he is under great\r\nobligation; that I confess, _dans mon gros bon sens_, I cannot\r\npass over. I am not exacting by nature, no, but there is a limit to\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnna Vassilyevna rang the bell in a tremor. A little page came in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why is it Pavel Yakovlitch does not come?\xe2\x80\x99 she said, \xe2\x80\x98what does it\r\nmean; I call him, and he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t come?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And what is the object, may I ask, of your wanting to send for him? I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t expect that at all, I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish it even!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s the object, Nikolai Artemyevitch? He has disturbed you; very\r\nlikely he has checked the progress of your cure. I want to have an\r\nexplanation with him. I want to know how he has dared to annoy you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I tell you again, that I do not ask that. And what can induce you ...\r\n_devant les domestiques_!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnna Vassilyevna flushed a little. \xe2\x80\x98You need not say that, Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch. I never... _devant les domestiques_... Fedushka, go and\r\nsee you bring Pavel Yakovlitch here at once.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe little page went off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And that\xe2\x80\x99s absolutely unnecessary,\xe2\x80\x99 muttered Nikolai Artemyevitch\r\nbetween his teeth, and he began again pacing up and down the room. \xe2\x80\x98I\r\ndid not bring up the subject with that object.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good Heavens, Paul must apologise to you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good Heavens, what are his apologies to me? And what do you mean by\r\napologies? That\xe2\x80\x99s all words.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, he must be corrected.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, you can correct him yourself. He will listen to you sooner than\r\nto me. For my part I bear him no grudge.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Nikolai Artemyevitch, you\xe2\x80\x99ve not been yourself ever since you\r\narrived. You have even to my eyes grown thinner lately. I am afraid your\r\ntreatment is doing you no good.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The treatment is quite indispensable,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Nikolai Artemyevitch,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98my liver is affected.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAt that instant Shubin came in. He looked tired. A slight almost\r\nironical smile played on his lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You asked for me, Anna Vassilyevna?\xe2\x80\x99 he observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, certainly I asked for you. Really, Paul, this is dreadful. I am\r\nvery much displeased with you. How could you be wanting in respect to\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nikolai Artemyevitch has complained of me to you?\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Shubin, and\r\nwith the same smile on his lips he looked at Stahov. The latter turned\r\naway, dropping his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, he complains of you. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you have done amiss, but\r\nyou ought to apologise at once, because his health is very much deranged\r\njust now, and indeed we all ought when we are young to treat our\r\nbenefactors with respect.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, what logic!\xe2\x80\x99 thought Shubin, and he turned to Stahov. \xe2\x80\x98I am ready\r\nto apologise to you, Nikolai Artemyevitch,\xe2\x80\x99 he said with a polite\r\nhalf-bow, \xe2\x80\x98if I have really offended you in any way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I did not at all... with that idea,\xe2\x80\x99 rejoined Nikolai Artemyevitch,\r\nstill as before avoiding Shubin\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. \xe2\x80\x98However, I will readily forgive\r\nyou, for, as you know, I am not an exacting person.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, that admits of no doubt!\xe2\x80\x99 said Shubin. \xe2\x80\x98But allow me to be\r\ninquisitive; is Anna Vassilyevna aware precisely what constituted my\r\noffence?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, I know nothing,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Anna Vassilyevna, craning forward her\r\nhead expectantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O Good Lord!\xe2\x80\x99 exclaimed Nikolai Artemyevitch hurriedly, \xe2\x80\x98how often have\r\nI prayed and besought, how often have I said how I hate these scenes\r\nand explanations! When one\xe2\x80\x99s been away an age, and comes home hoping for\r\nrest--talk of the family circle, _int\xc3\xa9rieur_, being a family man--and\r\nhere one finds scenes and unpleasantnesses. There\xe2\x80\x99s not a minute of\r\npeace. One\xe2\x80\x99s positively driven to the club... or, or elsewhere. A man is\r\nalive, he has a physical side, and it has its claims, but here----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnd without concluding his sentence Nikolai Artemyevitch went quickly\r\nout, slamming the door.\r\n\r\nAnna Vassilyevna looked after him. \xe2\x80\x98To the club!\xe2\x80\x99 she muttered bitterly:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98you are not going to the club, profligate? You\xe2\x80\x99ve no one at the club\r\nto give away my horses to--horses from my own stable--and the grey ones\r\ntoo! My favourite colour. Yes, yes, fickle-hearted man,\xe2\x80\x99 she went on\r\nraising her voice, \xe2\x80\x98you are not going to the club, As for you, Paul,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshe pursued, getting up, \xe2\x80\x98I wonder you\xe2\x80\x99re not ashamed. I should have\r\nthought you would not be so childish. And now my head has begun to ache.\r\nWhere is Zoya, do you know?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think she\xe2\x80\x99s upstairs in her room. The wise little fox always hides in\r\nher hole when there\xe2\x80\x99s a storm in the air.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come, please, please!\xe2\x80\x99 Anna Vassilyevna began searching about her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you seen my little glass of grated horse-radish? Paul, be so\r\ngood as not to make me angry for the future.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How make you angry, auntie? Give me your little hand to kiss. Your\r\nhorse-radish I saw on the little table in the boudoir.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Darya always leaves it about somewhere,\xe2\x80\x99 said Anna Vassilyevna, and she\r\nwalked away with a rustle of silk skirts.\r\n\r\nShubin was about to follow her, but he stopped on hearing Uvar\r\nIvanovitch\xe2\x80\x99s drawling voice behind him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I would... have given it you... young puppy,\xe2\x80\x99 the retired cornet\r\nbrought out in gasps.\r\n\r\nShubin went up to him. \xe2\x80\x98And what have I done, then, most venerable Uvar\r\nIvanovitch?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How! you are young, be respectful. Yes indeed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Respectful to whom?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To whom? You know whom. Ay, grin away.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin crossed his arms on his breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, you type of the choice element in drama,\xe2\x80\x99 he exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x98you\r\nprimeval force of the black earth, cornerstone of the social fabric!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nUvar Ivanovitch\xe2\x80\x99s fingers began to work. \xe2\x80\x98There, there, my boy, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nprovoke me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Shubin, \xe2\x80\x98is a gentleman, not young to judge by\r\nappearances, but what blissful, child-like faith is still hidden in\r\nhim! Respect! And do you know, you primitive creature, what Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch was in a rage with me for? Why I spent the whole of this\r\nmorning with him at his German woman\xe2\x80\x99s; we were singing the three of\r\nus--\xe2\x80\x9cDo not leave me.\xe2\x80\x9d You should have heard us--that would have moved\r\nyou. We sang and sang, my dear sir--and well, I got bored; I could see\r\nsomething was wrong, there was an alarming tenderness in the air. And\r\nI began to tease them both. I was very successful. First she was angry\r\nwith me, then with him; and then he got angry with her, and told her\r\nthat he was never happy except at home, and he had a paradise there; and\r\nshe told him he had no morals; and I murmured \xe2\x80\x9cAch!\xe2\x80\x9d to her in German.\r\nHe walked off and I stayed behind; he came here, to his paradise that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nto say, and he was soon sick of paradise, so he set to grumbling. Well\r\nnow, who do you consider was to blame?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You, of course,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Uvar Ivanovitch.\r\n\r\nShubin stared at him. \xe2\x80\x98May I venture to ask you, most reverend\r\nknight-errant,\xe2\x80\x99 he began in an obsequious voice, \xe2\x80\x98these enigmatical\r\nwords you have deigned to utter as the result of some exercise of your\r\nreflecting faculties, or under the influence of a momentary necessity to\r\nstart the vibration in the air known as sound?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t tempt me, I tell you,\xe2\x80\x99 groaned Uvar Ivanovitch.\r\n\r\nShubin laughed and ran away. \xe2\x80\x98Hi,\xe2\x80\x99 shouted Uvar Ivanovitch a quarter of\r\nan hour later, \xe2\x80\x98you there... a glass of spirits.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA little page brought the glass of spirits and some salt fish on a tray.\r\nUvar Ivanovitch slowly took the glass from the tray and gazed a long\r\nwhile with intense attention at it, as though he could not quite\r\nunderstand what it was he had in his hand. Then he looked at the page\r\nand asked him, \xe2\x80\x98Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t his name Vaska?\xe2\x80\x99 Then he assumed an air of\r\nresignation, drank off the spirit, munched the herring and was slowly\r\nproceeding to get his handkerchief out of his pocket. But the page had\r\nlong ago carried off and put away the tray and the decanter, eaten up\r\nthe remains of the herring and had time to go off to sleep, curled up in\r\na great-coat of his master\xe2\x80\x99s, while Uvar Ivanovitch still continued to\r\nhold the handkerchief before him in his opened fingers, and with the\r\nsame intense attention gazed now at the window, now at the floor and\r\nwalls.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIX\r\n\r\n\r\nShubin went back to his room in the lodge and was just opening a book,\r\nwhen Nikolai Artemyevitch\xe2\x80\x99s valet came cautiously into his room and\r\nhanded him a small triangular note, sealed with a thick heraldic crest.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope,\xe2\x80\x99 he found in the note, \xe2\x80\x98that you as a man of honour will\r\nnot allow yourself to hint by so much as a single word at a certain\r\npromissory note which was talked of this morning. You are acquainted\r\nwith my position and my rules, the insignificance of the sum in itself\r\nand the other circumstances; there are, in fine, family secrets which\r\nmust be respected, and family tranquillity is something so sacred that\r\nonly _\xc3\xaatres sans coeur_ (among whom I have no reason to reckon you) would\r\nrepudiate it! Give this note back to me.--N. S.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin scribbled below in pencil: \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t excite yourself, I\xe2\x80\x99m not quite\r\na sneak yet,\xe2\x80\x99 and gave the note back to the man, and again began\r\nupon the book. But it soon slipped out of his hands. He looked at the\r\nreddening sky, at the two mighty young pines standing apart from the\r\nother trees, thought \xe2\x80\x98by day pines are bluish, but how magnificently\r\ngreen they are in the evening,\xe2\x80\x99 and went out into the garden, in the\r\nsecret hope of meeting Elena there. He was not mistaken. Before him on a\r\npath between the bushes he caught a glimpse of her dress. He went after\r\nher, and when he was abreast with her, remarked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t look in my direction, I\xe2\x80\x99m not worth it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe gave him a cursory glance, smiled cursorily, and walked on further\r\ninto the depths of the garden. Shubin went after her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I beg you not to look at me,\xe2\x80\x99 he began, \xe2\x80\x98and then I address you;\r\nflagrant contradiction. But what of that? it\xe2\x80\x99s not the first time I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ncontradicted myself. I have just recollected that I have never begged\r\nyour pardon as I ought for my stupid behaviour yesterday. You are not\r\nangry with me, Elena Nikolaevna, are you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe stood still and did not answer him at once--not because she was\r\nangry, but because her thoughts were far away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No,\xe2\x80\x99 she said at last, \xe2\x80\x98I am not in the least angry.\xe2\x80\x99 Shubin bit his\r\nlip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What an absorbed... and what an indifferent face!\xe2\x80\x99 he muttered. \xe2\x80\x98Elena\r\nNikolaevna,\xe2\x80\x99 he continued, raising his voice, \xe2\x80\x98allow me to tell you a\r\nlittle anecdote. I had a friend, and this friend also had a friend, who\r\nat first conducted himself as befits a gentleman but afterwards took\r\nto drink. So one day early in the morning, my friend meets him in the\r\nstreet (and by that time, note, the acquaintance has been completely\r\ndropped) meets him and sees he is drunk. My friend went and turned his\r\nback on him. But he ran up and said, \xe2\x80\x9cI would not be angry,\xe2\x80\x9d says he,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cif you refused to recognise me, but why should you turn your back\r\non me? Perhaps I have been brought to this through grief. Peace to my\r\nashes!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin paused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And is that all?\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you. What are you hinting at? You told me just now\r\nnot to look your way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, and now I have told you that it\xe2\x80\x99s too bad to turn your back on\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But did I?\xe2\x80\x99 began Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did you not?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena flushed slightly and held out her hand to Shubin. He pressed it\r\nwarmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here you seem to have convicted me of a bad feeling,\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena, \xe2\x80\x98but\r\nyour suspicion is unjust. I was not even thinking of avoiding you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Granted, granted. But you must acknowledge that at that minute you had\r\na thousand ideas in your head of which you would not confide one to me.\r\nEh? I\xe2\x80\x99ve spoken the truth, I\xe2\x80\x99m quite sure?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps so.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And why is it? why?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My ideas are not clear to myself,\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then it\xe2\x80\x99s just the time for confiding them to some one else,\xe2\x80\x99 put in\r\nShubin. \xe2\x80\x98But I will tell you what it really is. You have a bad opinion\r\nof me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes you; you imagine that everything in me is half-humbug because I am\r\nan artist, that I am incapable not only of doing anything--in that you\r\nare very likely right--but even of any genuine deep feeling; you think\r\nthat I am not capable even of weeping sincerely, that I\xe2\x80\x99m a gossip and\r\na slanderer,--and all because I\xe2\x80\x99m an artist. What luckless, God-forsaken\r\nwretches we artists are after that! You, for instance, I am ready to\r\nadore, and you don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in my repentance.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Pavel Yakovlitch, I believe in your repentance and I believe in\r\nyour tears. But it seems to me that even your repentance amuses you--yes\r\nand your tears too.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin shuddered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, I see this is, as the doctors say, a hopeless case, _casus\r\nincurabilis_. There is nothing left but to bow the head and submit. And\r\nmeanwhile, good Heavens, can it be true, can I possibly be absorbed in\r\nmy own egoism when there is a soul like this living at my side? And to\r\nknow that one will never penetrate into that soul, never will know\r\nwhy it grieves and why it rejoices, what is working within it, what it\r\ndesires--whither it is going... Tell me,\xe2\x80\x99 he said after a short silence,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98could you never under any circumstances love an artist?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena looked straight into his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so, Pavel Yakovlitch; no.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Which was to be proved,\xe2\x80\x99 said Shubin with comical dejection. \xe2\x80\x98After\r\nwhich I suppose it would be more seemly for me not to intrude on your\r\nsolitary walk. A professor would ask you on what data you founded your\r\nanswer no. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a professor though, but a baby according to your\r\nideas; but one does not turn one\xe2\x80\x99s back on a baby, remember. Good-bye!\r\nPeace to my ashes!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena was on the point of stopping him, but after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s thought she\r\ntoo said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good-bye.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin went out of the courtyard. At a short distance from the Stahov\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse he was met by Bersenyev. He was walking with hurried steps, his\r\nhead bent and his hat pushed back on his neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Andrei Petrovitch!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Shubin.\r\n\r\nHe stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Go on, go on,\xe2\x80\x99 continued Shubin, \xe2\x80\x98I only shouted, I won\xe2\x80\x99t detain\r\nyou--and you\xe2\x80\x99d better slip straight into the garden--you\xe2\x80\x99ll find Elena\r\nthere, I fancy she\xe2\x80\x99s waiting for you... she\xe2\x80\x99s waiting for some one\r\nanyway.... Do you understand the force of those words: she is waiting!\r\nAnd do you know, my dear boy, an astonishing circumstance? Imagine, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntwo years now that I have been living in the same house with her, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nin love with her, and it\xe2\x80\x99s only just now, this minute, that I\xe2\x80\x99ve, not\r\nunderstood, but really seen her. I have seen her and I lifted up my\r\nhands in amazement. Don\xe2\x80\x99t look at me, please, with that sham sarcastic\r\nsmile, which does not suit your sober features. Well, now, I suppose you\r\nwant to remind me of Annushka. What of it? I don\xe2\x80\x99t deny it. Annushkas\r\nare on my poor level. And long life to all Annushkas and Zoyas and even\r\nAugustina Christianovnas! You go to Elena now, and I will make my way\r\nto--Annushka, you fancy? No, my dear fellow, worse than that; to Prince\r\nTchikurasov. He is a Maecenas of a Kazan-Tartar stock, after the style\r\nof Volgin. Do you see this note of invitation, these letters, R.S.V.P.?\r\nEven in the country there\xe2\x80\x99s no peace for me. Addio!\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev listened\r\nto Shubin\xe2\x80\x99s tirade in silence, looking as though he were just a little\r\nashamed of him. Then he went into the courtyard of the Stahovs\xe2\x80\x99 house.\r\nAnd Shubin did really go to Prince Tchikurasov, to whom with the most\r\ncordial air he began saying the most insulting things. The Maecenas of\r\nthe Tartars of Kazan chuckled; the Maecenas\xe2\x80\x99s guests laughed, but no one\r\nfelt merry, and every one was in a bad temper when the party broke up.\r\nSo two gentlemen slightly acquainted may be seen when they meet on the\r\nNevsky Prospect suddenly grinning at one another and pursing up their\r\neyes and noses and cheeks, and then, directly they have passed one\r\nanother, they resume their former indifferent, often cross, and\r\ngenerally sickly, expression.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\n\r\nElena met Bersenyev cordially, though not in the garden, but the\r\ndrawing-room, and at once, almost impatiently, renewed the conversation\r\nof the previous day. She was alone; Nikolai Artemyevitch had quietly\r\nslipped away. Anna Vassilyevna was lying down upstairs with a wet\r\nbandage on her head. Zoya was sitting by her, the folds of her skirt\r\narranged precisely about her, and her little hands clasped on her knees.\r\nUvar Ivanovitch was reposing in the attic on a wide and comfortable\r\ndivan, known as a \xe2\x80\x98samo-son\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98dozer.\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev again mentioned his\r\nfather; he held his memory sacred. Let us, too, say a few words about\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nThe owner of eighty-two serfs, whom he set free before his death, an\r\nold Gottingen student, and disciple of the \xe2\x80\x98Illuminati,\xe2\x80\x99 the author of\r\na manuscript work on \xe2\x80\x98transformations or typifications of the spirit in\r\nthe world\xe2\x80\x99--a work in which Schelling\xe2\x80\x99s philosophy, Swedenborgianism\r\nand republicanism were mingled in the most original fashion--Bersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfather brought him, while still a boy, to Moscow immediately after his\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s death, and at once himself undertook his education. He\r\nprepared himself for each lesson, exerted himself with extraordinary\r\nconscientiousness and absolute lack of success: he was a dreamer, a\r\nbookworm, and a mystic; he spoke in a dull, hesitating voice, used\r\nobscure and roundabout expressions, metaphorical by preference, and was\r\nshy even of his son, whom he loved passionately. It was not surprising\r\nthat his son was simply bewildered at his lessons, and did not advance\r\nin the least. The old man (he was almost fifty, he had married late in\r\nlife) surmised at last that things were not going quite right, and he\r\nplaced his Andrei in a school. Andrei began to learn, but he was\r\nnot removed from his father\xe2\x80\x99s supervision; his father visited him\r\nunceasingly, wearying the schoolmaster to death with his instructions\r\nand conversation; the teachers, too, were bored by his uninvited visits;\r\nhe was for ever bringing them some, as they said, far-fetched books on\r\neducation. Even the schoolboys were embarrassed at the sight of the old\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s swarthy, pockmarked face, his lank figure, invariably clothed in\r\na sort of scanty grey dresscoat. The boys did not suspect then that this\r\ngrim, unsmiling old gentleman, with his crane-like gait and his long\r\nnose, was at heart troubling and yearning over each one of them almost\r\nas over his own son. He once conceived the idea of talking to them about\r\nWashington: \xe2\x80\x98My young nurslings,\xe2\x80\x99 he began, but at the first sounds of\r\nhis strange voice the young nurslings ran away. The good old Gottingen\r\nstudent did not lie on a bed of roses; he was for ever weighed down by\r\nthe march of history, by questions and ideas of every kind. When young\r\nBersenyev entered the university, his father used to drive with him\r\nto the lectures, but his health was already beginning to break up. The\r\nevents of the year 1848 shook him to the foundation (it necessitated the\r\nre-writing of his whole book), and he died in the winter of 1853, before\r\nhis son\xe2\x80\x99s time at the university was over, but he was able beforehand to\r\ncongratulate him on his degree, and to consecrate him to the service of\r\nscience. \xe2\x80\x98I pass on the torch to you,\xe2\x80\x99 he said to him two hours before\r\nhis death. \xe2\x80\x98I held it while I could; you, too, must not let the light\r\ngrow dim before the end.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev talked a long while to Elena of his father. The embarrassment\r\nhe had felt in her presence disappeared, and his lisp was less marked.\r\nThe conversation passed on to the university.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell me,\xe2\x80\x99 Elena asked him, \xe2\x80\x98were there any remarkable men among your\r\ncomrades?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev was again reminded of Shubin\xe2\x80\x99s words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Elena Nikolaevna, to tell you the truth, there was not a single\r\nremarkable man among us. And, indeed, where are such to be found! There\r\nwas, they say, a good time once in the Moscow university! But not now.\r\nNow it\xe2\x80\x99s a school, not a university. I was not happy with my comrades,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhe added, dropping his voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not happy,\xe2\x80\x99 murmured Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But I ought,\xe2\x80\x99 continued Bersenyev, \xe2\x80\x98to make an exception. I know one\r\nstudent--it\xe2\x80\x99s true he is not in the same faculty--he is certainly a\r\nremarkable man.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is his name?\xe2\x80\x99 Elena inquired with interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Insarov Dmitri Nikanorovitch. He is a Bulgarian.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not a Russian?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, he is not a Russian,\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why is he living in Moscow, then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He came here to study. And do you know with what aim he is studying?\r\nHe has a single idea: the liberation of his country. And his story is\r\nan exceptional one. His father was a fairly well-to-do merchant; he came\r\nfrom Tirnova. Tirnova is now a small town, but it was the capital of\r\nBulgaria in the old days when Bulgaria was still an independent state.\r\nHe traded with Sophia, and had relations with Russia; his sister,\r\nInsarov\xe2\x80\x99s aunt, is still living in Kiev, married to a senior history\r\nteacher in the gymnasium there. In 1835, that is to say eighteen\r\nyears ago, a terrible crime was committed; Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s mother suddenly\r\ndisappeared without leaving a trace behind; a week later she was found\r\nmurdered.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena shuddered. Bersenyev stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Go on, go on,\xe2\x80\x99 she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There were rumours that she had been outraged and murdered by a Turkish\r\naga; her husband, Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s father, found out the truth, tried to avenge\r\nher, but only succeeded in wounding the aga with his poniard.... He was\r\nshot.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Shot, and without a trial?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes. Insarov was just eight years old at the time. He remained in\r\nthe hands of neighbours. The sister heard of the fate of her brother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfamily, and wanted to take the nephew to live with her. They got him\r\nto Odessa, and from there to Kiev. At Kiev he lived twelve whole years.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s how it is he speaks Russian so well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He speaks Russian?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Just as we do. When he was twenty (that was at the beginning of the\r\nyear 1848) he began to want to return to his country. He stayed in\r\nSophia and Tirnova, and travelled through the length and breadth of\r\nBulgaria, spending two years there, and learning his mother tongue\r\nover again. The Turkish Government persecuted him, and he was certainly\r\nexposed to great dangers during those two years; I once caught sight of\r\na broad scar on his neck, from a wound, no doubt; but he does not like\r\nto talk about it. He is reserved, too, in his own way. I have tried to\r\nquestion him about everything, but I could get nothing out of him. He\r\nanswers by generalities. He\xe2\x80\x99s awfully obstinate. He returned to Russia\r\nagain in 1850, to Moscow, with the intention of educating himself\r\nthoroughly, getting intimate with Russians, and then when he leaves the\r\nuniversity----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What then?\xe2\x80\x99 broke in Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What God wills. It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to forecast the future.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nFor a while Elena did not take her eyes off Bersenyev.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have greatly interested me by what you have told me,\xe2\x80\x99 she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is he like, this friend of yours; what did you call him, Insarov?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What shall I say? To my mind, he\xe2\x80\x99s good-looking. But you will see him\r\nfor yourself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How so?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will bring him here to see you. He is coming to our little village\r\nthe day after tomorrow, and is going to live with me in the same\r\nlodging.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Really? But will he care to come to see us?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I should think so. He will be delighted.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He isn\xe2\x80\x99t proud, then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not the least. That\xe2\x80\x99s to say, he is proud if you like, only not in the\r\nsense you mean. He will never, for instance, borrow money from any one.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is he poor?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, he isn\xe2\x80\x99t rich. When he went to Bulgaria he collected some relics\r\nleft of his father\xe2\x80\x99s property, and his aunt helps him; but it all comes\r\nto very little.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He must have a great deal of character,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes. He is a man of iron. And at the same time you will see there is\r\nsomething childlike and frank, with all his concentration and even his\r\nreserve. It\xe2\x80\x99s true, his frankness is not our poor sort of frankness--the\r\nfrankness of people who have absolutely nothing to conceal.... But\r\nthere, I will bring him to see you; wait a little.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And isn\xe2\x80\x99t he shy?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Elena again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, he\xe2\x80\x99s not shy. It\xe2\x80\x99s only vain people who are shy.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, are you vain?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe was confused and made a vague gesture with his hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You excite my curiosity,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Elena. \xe2\x80\x98But tell me, has he not taken\r\nvengeance on that Turkish aga?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev smiled\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Revenge is only to be found in novels, Elena Nikolaevna; and, besides,\r\nin twelve years that aga may well be dead.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Insarov has never said anything, though, to you about it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, never.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why did he go to Sophia?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98His father used to live there.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena grew thoughtful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To liberate one\xe2\x80\x99s country!\xe2\x80\x99 she said. \xe2\x80\x98It is terrible even to utter\r\nthose words, they are so grand.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAt that instant Anna Vassilyevna came into the room, and the\r\nconversation stopped.\r\n\r\nBersenyev was stirred by strange emotions when he returned home that\r\nevening. He did not regret his plan of making Elena acquainted with\r\nInsarov, he felt the deep impression made on her by his account of the\r\nyoung Bulgarian very natural... had he not himself tried to deepen that\r\nimpression! But a vague, unfathomable emotion lurked secretly in his\r\nheart; he was sad with a sadness that had nothing noble in it. This\r\nsadness did not prevent him, however, from setting to work on the\r\n_History of the Hohenstaufen_, and beginning to read it at the very page\r\nat which he had left off the evening before.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\n\r\nTwo days later, Insarov in accordance with his promise arrived at\r\nBersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s with his luggage. He had no servant; but without any\r\nassistance he put his room to rights, arranged the furniture, dusted and\r\nswept the floor. He had special trouble with the writing table, which\r\nwould not fit into the recess in the wall assigned for it; but Insarov,\r\nwith the silent persistence peculiar to him succeeded in getting his own\r\nway with it. When he had settled in, he asked Bersenyev to let him pay\r\nhim ten roubles in advance, and arming himself with a thick stick, set\r\noff to inspect the country surrounding his new abode. He returned three\r\nhours later; and in response to Bersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s invitation to share his\r\nrepast, he said that he would not refuse to dine with him that day, but\r\nthat he had already spoken to the woman of the house, and would get her\r\nto send him up his meals for the future.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Upon my word!\xe2\x80\x99 said Bersenyev, \xe2\x80\x98you will fare very badly; that old body\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t cook a bit. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you dine with me, we would go halves over\r\nthe cost.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My means don\xe2\x80\x99t allow me to dine as you do,\xe2\x80\x99 Insarov replied with a\r\ntranquil smile.\r\n\r\nThere was something in that smile which forbade further insistence;\r\nBersenyev did not add a word. After dinner he proposed to Insarov that\r\nhe should take him to the Stahovs; but he replied that he had intended\r\nto devote the evening to correspondence with his Bulgarians, and so\r\nhe would ask him to put off the visit to the Stahovs till next day.\r\nBersenyev was already familiar with Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s unbending will; but it\r\nwas only now when he was under the same roof with him, that he fully\r\nrealised at last that Insarov would never alter any decision, just in\r\nthe same way as he would never fail to carry out a promise he had\r\ngiven; to Bersenyev--a Russian to his fingertips--this more than German\r\nexactitude seemed at first odd, and even rather ludicrous; but he soon\r\ngot used to it, and ended by finding it--if not deserving of respect--at\r\nleast very convenient.\r\n\r\nThe second day after his arrival, Insarov got up at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the\r\nmorning, made a round of almost all Kuntsovo, bathed in the river, drank\r\na glass of cold milk, and then set to work. And he had plenty of work\r\nto do; he was studying Russian history and law, and political economy,\r\ntranslating the Bulgarian ballads and chronicles, collecting materials\r\non the Eastern Question, and compiling a Russian grammar for the use of\r\nBulgarians, and a Bulgarian grammar for the use of Russians. Bersenyev\r\nwent up to him and began to discuss Feuerbach. Insarov listened\r\nattentively, made few remarks, but to the point; it was clear from his\r\nobservations that he was trying to arrive at a conclusion as to whether\r\nhe need study Feuerbach, or whether he could get on without him.\r\nBersenyev turned the conversation on to his pursuits, and asked him if\r\nhe could not show him anything. Insarov read him his translation of two\r\nor three Bulgarian ballads, and was anxious to hear his opinion of them.\r\nBersenyev thought the translation a faithful one, but not sufficiently\r\nspirited. Insarov paid close attention to his criticism. From the\r\nballads Bersenyev passed on to the present position of Bulgaria, and\r\nthen for the first time he noticed what a change came over Insarov at\r\nthe mere mention of his country: not that his face flushed nor his\r\nvoice grew louder--no! but at once a sense of force and intense onward\r\nstriving was expressed in his whole personality, the lines of his mouth\r\ngrew harder and less flexible, and a dull persistent fire glowed in the\r\ndepths of his eyes. Insarov did not care to enlarge on his own travels\r\nin his country; but of Bulgaria in general he talked readily with any\r\none. He talked at length of the Turks, of their oppression, of\r\nthe sorrows and disasters of his countrymen, and of their hopes:\r\nconcentrated meditation on a single ruling passion could be heard in\r\nevery word he uttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, well, there\xe2\x80\x99s no mistake about it,\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev was reflecting\r\nmeanwhile, \xe2\x80\x98that Turkish aga, I venture to think, has been punished for\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s and mother\xe2\x80\x99s death.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov had not had time to say all he wanted to say, when the door\r\nopened and Shubin made his appearance.\r\n\r\nHe came into the room with an almost exaggerated air of ease and\r\ngood-humour; Bersenyev, who knew him well, could see at once that\r\nsomething had been jarring on him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will introduce myself without ceremony,\xe2\x80\x99 he began with a bright and\r\nopen expression on his face. \xe2\x80\x98My name is Shubin; I\xe2\x80\x99m a friend of this\r\nyoung man here\xe2\x80\x99 (he indicated Bersenyev). \xe2\x80\x98You are Mr. Insarov, of\r\ncourse, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am Insarov.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then give me your hand and let us be friends. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know if Bersenyev\r\nhas talked to you about me, but he has told me a great deal about you.\r\nYou are staying here? Capital! Don\xe2\x80\x99t be offended at my staring at you\r\nso. I\xe2\x80\x99m a sculptor by trade, and I foresee I shall in a little time be\r\nbegging your permission to model your head.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My head\xe2\x80\x99s at your service,\xe2\x80\x99 said Insarov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What shall we do to-day, eh?\xe2\x80\x99 began Shubin, sitting down suddenly on a\r\nlow chair, with his knees apart and his elbows propped on them. \xe2\x80\x98Andrei\r\nPetrovitch, has your honour any kind of plan for to-day? It\xe2\x80\x99s glorious\r\nweather; there\xe2\x80\x99s a scent of hay and dried strawberries as if one were\r\ndrinking strawberry-tea for a cold. We ought to get up some kind of\r\na spree. Let us show the new inhabitant of Kuntsov all its numerous\r\nbeauties.\xe2\x80\x99 (Something has certainly upset him, Bersenyev kept thinking\r\nto himself.) \xe2\x80\x98Well, why art thou silent, friend Horatio? Open your\r\nprophetic lips. Shall we go off on a spree, or not?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how Insarov feels,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98He is just\r\ngetting to work, I fancy.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin turned round on his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You want to work?\xe2\x80\x99 he inquired, in a somewhat condescending voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Insarov; \xe2\x80\x98to-day I could give up to walking.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah!\xe2\x80\x99 commented Shubin. \xe2\x80\x98Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s delightful. Run along, my friend,\r\nAndrei Petrovitch, put a hat on your learned head, and let us go where\r\nour eyes lead us. Our eyes are young--they may lead us far. I know a\r\nvery repulsive little restaurant, where they will give us a very beastly\r\nlittle dinner; but we shall be very jolly. Come along.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHalf an hour later they were all three walking along the bank of the\r\nMoskva. Insarov had a rather queer cap with flaps, over which Shubin\r\nfell into not very spontaneous raptures. Insarov walked without\r\nhaste, and looked about, breathing, talking, and smiling with the same\r\ntranquillity; he was giving this day up to pleasure, and enjoying it\r\nto the utmost. \xe2\x80\x98Just as well-behaved boys walk out on Sundays,\xe2\x80\x99 Shubin\r\nwhispered in Bersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s ear. Shubin himself played the fool a great\r\ndeal, ran in front, threw himself into the attitudes of famous statues,\r\nand turned somersaults on the grass; Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s tranquillity did not\r\nexactly irritate him, but it spurred him on to playing antics. \xe2\x80\x98What\r\na fidget you are, Frenchman!\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev said twice to him. \xe2\x80\x98Yes, I am\r\nFrench, half French,\xe2\x80\x99 Shubin answered, \xe2\x80\x98and you hold the happy medium\r\nbetween jest and earnest, as a waiter once said to me.\xe2\x80\x99 The young men\r\nturned away from the river and went along a deep and narrow ravine\r\nbetween two walls of tall golden rye; a bluish shadow was cast on them\r\nfrom the rye on one side; the flashing sunlight seemed to glide over the\r\ntops of the ears; the larks were singing, the quails were calling: on\r\nall sides was the brilliant green of the grass; a warm breeze stirred\r\nand lifted the leaves and shook the heads of the flowers. After\r\nprolonged wanderings, with rest and chat between (Shubin had even tried\r\nto play leap-frog with a toothless peasant they met, who did nothing but\r\nlaugh, whatever the gentlemen might do to him), the young men reached\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x98repulsive little\xe2\x80\x99 restaurant: the waiter almost knocked each of\r\nthem over, and did really provide them with a very bad dinner with a\r\nsort of Balkan wine, which did not, however, prevent them from being\r\nvery jolly, as Shubin had foretold; he himself was the loudest and the\r\nleast jolly. He drank to the health of the incomprehensible but great\r\n_Venelin_, the health of the Bulgarian king Kuma, Huma, or Hroma, who\r\nlived somewhere about the time of Adam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In the ninth century,\xe2\x80\x99 Insarov corrected him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In the ninth century?\xe2\x80\x99 cried Shubin. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, how delightful!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev noticed that among all his pranks, and jests and gaiety,\r\nShubin was constantly, as it were, examining Insarov; he was sounding\r\nhim and was in inward excitement, but Insarov remained as before, calm\r\nand straightforward.\r\n\r\nAt last they returned home, changed their dress, and resolved to finish\r\nthe day as they had begun it, by going that evening to the Stahovs.\r\nShubin ran on before them to announce their arrival.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXII\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The conquering hero Insarov will be here directly!\xe2\x80\x99 he shouted\r\ntriumphantly, going into the Stahovs\xe2\x80\x99 drawing-room, where there happened\r\nat the instant to be only Elena and Zoya.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_Wer_?\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Zoya in German. When she was taken unawares she always\r\nused her native language. Elena drew herself up. Shubin looked at her\r\nwith a playful smile on his lips. She felt annoyed, but said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You heard,\xe2\x80\x99 he repeated, \xe2\x80\x98Mr. Insarov is coming here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I heard,\xe2\x80\x99 she replied; \xe2\x80\x98and I heard how you spoke of him. I am\r\nsurprised at you, indeed. Mr. Insarov has not yet set foot in the house,\r\nand you already think fit to turn him into ridicule.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin was crestfallen at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are right, you are always right, Elena Nikolaevna,\xe2\x80\x99 he muttered;\r\n\xe2\x80\x98but I meant nothing, on my honour. We have been walking together with\r\nhim the whole day, and he\xe2\x80\x99s a capital fellow, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I didn\xe2\x80\x99t ask your opinion about that,\xe2\x80\x99 commented Elena, getting up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is Mr. Insarov a young man?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Zoya.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He is a hundred and forty-four,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Shubin with an air of\r\nvexation.\r\n\r\nThe page announced the arrival of the two friends. They came in.\r\nBersenyev introduced Insarov. Elena asked them to sit down, and sat\r\ndown herself, while Zoya went off upstairs; she had to inform Anna\r\nVassilyevna of their arrival. A conversation was begun of a rather\r\ninsignificant kind, like all first conversations. Shubin was silently\r\nwatching from a corner, but there was nothing to watch. In Elena he\r\ndetected signs of repressed annoyance against him--Shubin--and that was\r\nall. He looked at Bersenyev and at Insarov, and compared their\r\nfaces from a sculptor\xe2\x80\x99s point of view. \xe2\x80\x98They are neither of them\r\ngood-looking,\xe2\x80\x99 he thought, \xe2\x80\x98the Bulgarian has a characteristic\r\nface--there now it\xe2\x80\x99s in a good light; the Great-Russian is better\r\nadapted for painting; there are no lines, there\xe2\x80\x99s expression. But, I\r\ndare say, one might fall in love with either of them. She is not in love\r\nyet, but she will fall in love with Bersenyev,\xe2\x80\x99 he decided to himself.\r\nAnna Vassilyevna made her appearance in the drawing-room, and\r\nthe conversation took the tone peculiar to summer villas--not the\r\ncountry-house tone but the peculiar summer visitor tone. It was a\r\nconversation diversified by plenty of subjects; but broken by short\r\nrather wearisome pauses every three minutes. In one of these pauses Anna\r\nVassilyevna turned to Zoya. Shubin understood her silent hint, and drew\r\na long face, while Zoya sat down to the piano, and played and sang all\r\nher pieces through. Uvar Ivanovitch showed himself for an instant in the\r\ndoorway, but he beat a retreat, convulsively twitching his fingers. Then\r\ntea was served; and then the whole party went out into the garden.... It\r\nbegan to grow dark outside, and the guests took leave.\r\n\r\nInsarov had really made less impression on Elena than she had expected,\r\nor, speaking more exactly, he had not made the impression she had\r\nexpected. She liked his directness and unconstraint, and she liked his\r\nface; but the whole character of Insarov--with his calm firmness and\r\neveryday simplicity--did not somehow accord with the image formed in her\r\nbrain by Bersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s account of him. Elena, though she did not\r\nherself suspect it, had anticipated something more fateful. \xe2\x80\x98But,\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nreflected, \xe2\x80\x98he spoke very little to-day, and I am myself to blame for\r\nit; I did not question him, we must have patience till next time...\r\nand his eyes are expressive, honest eyes.\xe2\x80\x99 She felt that she had no\r\ndisposition to humble herself before him, but rather to hold out her\r\nhand to him in friendly equality, and she was puzzled; this was not how\r\nshe had fancied men, like Insarov, \xe2\x80\x98heroes.\xe2\x80\x99 This last word reminded her\r\nof Shubin, and she grew hot and angry, as she lay in her bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How did you like your new acquaintances?\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev inquired of Insarov\r\non their way home.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I liked them very much,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Insarov, \xe2\x80\x98especially the daughter.\r\nShe must be a nice girl. She is excitable, but in her it\xe2\x80\x99s a fine kind\r\nof excitability.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You must go and see them a little oftener,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Bersenyev.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, I must,\xe2\x80\x99 said Insarov; and he said nothing more all the way home.\r\nHe at once shut himself up in his room, but his candle was burning long\r\nafter midnight.\r\n\r\nBersenyev had had time to read a page of Raumer, when a handful of fine\r\ngravel came rattling on his window-pane. He could not help starting;\r\nopening the window he saw Shubin as white as a sheet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What an irrepressible fellow you are, you night moth----\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev was\r\nbeginning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sh--\xe2\x80\x99 Shubin cut him short; \xe2\x80\x98I have come to you in secret, as Max went\r\nto Agatha I absolutely must say a few words to you alone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come into the room then.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, that\xe2\x80\x99s not necessary,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Shubin, and he leaned his elbows\r\non the window-sill, \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s better fun like this, more as if we were in\r\nSpain. To begin with, I congratulate you, you\xe2\x80\x99re at a premium now. Your\r\nbelauded, exceptional man has quite missed fire. That I\xe2\x80\x99ll guarantee.\r\nAnd to prove my impartiality, listen--here\xe2\x80\x99s the sum and substance of\r\nMr. Insarov. No talents, none, no poetry, any amount of capacity for\r\nwork, an immense memory, an intellect not deep nor varied, but sound\r\nand quick, dry as dust, and force, and even the gift of the gab when the\r\ntalk\xe2\x80\x99s about his--between ourselves let it be said--tedious Bulgaria.\r\nWhat! do you say I am unjust? One remark more: you\xe2\x80\x99ll never come to\r\nChristian names with him, and none ever has been on such terms with him.\r\nI, of course, as an artist, am hateful to him; and I am proud of it. Dry\r\nas dust, dry as dust, but he can crush all of us to powder. He\xe2\x80\x99s devoted\r\nto his country--not like our empty patriots who fawn on the people; pour\r\ninto us, they say, thou living water! But, of course, his problem is\r\neasier, more intelligible: he has only to drive the Turks out, a mighty\r\ntask. But all these qualities, thank God, don\xe2\x80\x99t please women. There\xe2\x80\x99s no\r\nfascination, no charm about them, as there is about you and me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why do you bring me in?\xe2\x80\x99 muttered Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98And you are wrong in\r\nall the rest; you are not in the least hateful to him, and with his own\r\ncountrymen he is on Christian name terms--that I know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s a different matter! For them he\xe2\x80\x99s a hero; but, to make a\r\nconfession, I have a very different idea of a hero; a hero ought not to\r\nbe able to talk; a hero should roar like a bull, but when he butts with\r\nhis horns, the walls shake. He ought not to know himself why he butts at\r\nthings, but just to butt at them. But, perhaps, in our days heroes of a\r\ndifferent stamp are needed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why are you so taken up with Insarov?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98Can you have\r\nrun here only to describe his character to me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I came here,\xe2\x80\x99 began Shubin, \xe2\x80\x98because I was very miserable at home.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, that\xe2\x80\x99s it! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you want to have a cry again?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You may laugh! I came here because I\xe2\x80\x99m at my wits\xe2\x80\x99 end, because I am\r\ndevoured by despair, anger, jealousy.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jealousy? of whom?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of you and him and every one. I\xe2\x80\x99m tortured by the thought that if I had\r\nunderstood her sooner, if I had set to work cleverly--But what\xe2\x80\x99s the use\r\nof talking! It must end by my always laughing, playing the fool, turning\r\nthings into ridicule as she says, and then setting to and strangling\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Stuff, you won\xe2\x80\x99t strangle yourself,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Bersenyev.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98On such a night, of course not; but only let me live on till the\r\nautumn. On such a night people do die too, but only of happiness. Ah,\r\nhappiness! Every shadow that stretches across the road from every tree\r\nseems whispering now: \xe2\x80\x9cI know where there is happiness... shall I tell\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d I would ask you to come for a walk, only now you\xe2\x80\x99re under the\r\ninfluence of prose. Go to sleep, and may your dreams be visited by\r\nmathematical figures! My heart is breaking. You, worthy gentlemen, see\r\na man laughing, and that means to your notions he\xe2\x80\x99s all right; you can\r\nprove to him that he\xe2\x80\x99s humbugging himself, that\xe2\x80\x99s to say, he is not\r\nsuffering.... God bless you!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin abruptly left the window. \xe2\x80\x98Annu-shka!\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev felt an impulse\r\nto shout after him, but he restrained himself; Shubin had really been\r\nwhite with emotion. Two minutes later, Bersenyev even caught the sound\r\nof sobbing; he got up and opened the window; everything was still, only\r\nsomewhere in the distance some one--a passing peasant, probably--was\r\nhumming \xe2\x80\x98The Plain of Mozdok.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nDuring the first fortnight of Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s stay in the Kuntsovo\r\nneighbourhood, he did not visit the Stahovs more than four or five\r\ntimes; Bersenyev went to see them every day. Elena was always pleased to\r\nsee him, lively and interesting talk always sprang up between them,\r\nand yet he often went home with a gloomy face. Shubin scarcely showed\r\nhimself; he was working with feverish energy at his art; he either\r\nstayed locked up in his room, from which he would emerge in a blouse,\r\nsmeared all over with clay, or else he spent days in Moscow where he\r\nhad a studio, to which models and Italian sculptors, his friends and\r\nteachers, used to come to see him. Elena did not once succeed in talking\r\nwith Insarov, as she would have liked to do; in his absence she prepared\r\nquestions to ask him about many things, but when he came she felt\r\nashamed of her plans. Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s very tranquillity embarrassed her; it\r\nseemed to her that she had not the right to force him to speak out; and\r\nshe resolved to wait; for all that, she felt that at every visit however\r\ntrivial might be the words that passed between them, he attracted her\r\nmore and more; but she never happened to be left alone with him--and\r\nto grow intimate with any one, one must have at least one conversation\r\nalone with him. She talked a great deal about him to Bersenyev.\r\nBersenyev realised that Elena\xe2\x80\x99s imagination had been struck by Insarov,\r\nand was glad that his friend had not \xe2\x80\x98missed fire\xe2\x80\x99 as Shubin had\r\nasserted. He told her cordially all he knew of him down to the minutest\r\ndetails (we often, when we want to please some one, bring our friends\r\ninto our conversation, hardly ever suspecting that we are praising\r\nourselves in that way), and only at times, when Elena\xe2\x80\x99s pale cheeks\r\nflushed a little and her eyes grew bright and wide, he felt a pang in\r\nhis heart of that evil pain which he had felt before.\r\n\r\nOne day Bersenyev came to the Stahovs, not at the customary time, but at\r\neleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning. Elena came down to him in the parlour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Fancy,\xe2\x80\x99 he began with a constrained smile, \xe2\x80\x98our Insarov has\r\ndisappeared.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Disappeared?\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has disappeared. The day before yesterday he went off somewhere and\r\nnothing has been seen of him since.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He did not tell you where he was going?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena sank into a chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has most likely gone to Moscow,\xe2\x80\x99 she commented, trying to seem\r\nindifferent and at the same time wondering that she should try to seem\r\nindifferent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x99 rejoined Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98He did not go alone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98With whom then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Two people of some sort--his countrymen they must have been--came to\r\nhim the day before yesterday, before dinner.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bulgarians! what makes you think so?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why as far as I could hear, they talked to him in some language I did\r\nnot know, but Slavonic... You are always saying, Elena Nikolaevna, that\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s so little mystery about Insarov; what could be more mysterious\r\nthan this visit? Imagine, they came to him--and then there was shouting\r\nand quarrelling, and such savage, angry disputing.... And he shouted\r\ntoo.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He shouted too?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes. He shouted at them. They seemed to be accusing each other. And\r\nif you could have had a peep at these visitors. They had swarthy, heavy\r\nfaces with high cheek bones and hook noses, both about forty years old,\r\nshabbily dressed, hot and dusty, looking like workmen--not workmen, and\r\nnot gentlemen--goodness knows what sort of people they were.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And he went away with them?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes. He gave them something to eat and went off with them. The woman of\r\nthe house told me they ate a whole huge pot of porridge between the\r\ntwo of them. They outdid one another, she said, and gobbled it up like\r\nwolves.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena gave a faint smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You will see,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, \xe2\x80\x98all this will be explained into something\r\nvery prosaic.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope it may! But you need not use that word. There is nothing prosaic\r\nabout Insarov, though Shubin does maintain----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Shubin!\xe2\x80\x99 Elena broke in, shrugging her shoulders. \xe2\x80\x98But you must confess\r\nthese two good men gobbling up porridge----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Even Themistocles had his supper on the eve of Salamis,\xe2\x80\x99 observed\r\nBersenyev with a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes; but then there was a battle next day. Any way you will let me know\r\nwhen he comes back,\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena, and she tried to change the subject,\r\nbut the conversation made little progress. Zoya made her appearance\r\nand began walking about the room on tip-toe, giving them thereby to\r\nunderstand that Anna Vassilyevna was not yet awake.\r\n\r\nBersenyev went away.\r\n\r\nIn the evening of the same day a note from him was brought to Elena.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has come back,\xe2\x80\x99 he wrote to her, \xe2\x80\x98sunburnt and dusty to his very\r\neyebrows; but where and why he went I don\xe2\x80\x99t know; won\xe2\x80\x99t you find out?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Won\xe2\x80\x99t you find out!\xe2\x80\x99 Elena whispered, \xe2\x80\x98as though he talked to me!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next day, at two o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Elena was standing in the garden before a\r\nsmall kennel, where she was rearing two puppies. (A gardener had found\r\nthem deserted under a hedge, and brought them to the young mistress,\r\nbeing told by the laundry-maids that she took pity on beasts of\r\nall sorts. He was not wrong in his reckoning. Elena had given him a\r\nquarter-rouble.) She looked into the kennel, assured herself that the\r\npuppies were alive and well, and that they had been provided with fresh\r\nstraw, turned round, and almost uttered a cry; down an alley straight\r\ntowards her was walking Insarov, alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good-morning,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, coming up to her and taking off his cap. She\r\nnoticed that he certainly had got much sunburnt during the last three\r\ndays. \xe2\x80\x98I meant to have come here with Andrei Petrovitch, but he was\r\nrather slow in starting; so here I am without him. There is no one in\r\nyour house; they are all asleep or out of doors, so I came on here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You seem to be apologising,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Elena. \xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s no need to do\r\nthat. We are always very glad to see you. Let us sit here on the bench\r\nin the shade.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe seated herself. Insarov sat down near her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have not been at home these last days, I think?\xe2\x80\x99 she began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No,\xe2\x80\x99 he answered. \xe2\x80\x98I went away. Did Andrei Petrovitch tell you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov looked at her, smiled, and began playing with his cap. When he\r\nsmiled, his eyes blinked, and his lips puckered up, which gave him a\r\nvery good-humoured appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Andrei Petrovitch most likely told you too that I went away with\r\nsome--unattractive people,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, still smiling.\r\n\r\nElena was a little confused, but she felt at once that Insarov must\r\nalways be told the truth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 she said decisively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What did you think of me?\xe2\x80\x99 he asked her suddenly.\r\n\r\nElena raised her eyes to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, \xe2\x80\x98I thought that you always know what you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ndoing, and you are incapable of doing anything wrong.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well--thanks for that. You see, Elena Nikolaevna,\xe2\x80\x99 he began, coming\r\ncloser to her in a confidential way, \xe2\x80\x98there is a little family of our\r\npeople here; among us there are men of little culture; but all are\r\nwarmly devoted to the common cause. Unluckily, one can never get on\r\nwithout dissensions, and they all know me, and trust me; so they sent\r\nfor me to settle a dispute. I went.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Was it far from here?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I went about fifty miles, to the Troitsky district. There, near the\r\nmonastery, there are some of our people. At any rate, my trouble was not\r\nthrown away; I settled the matter.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And had you much difficulty?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes. One was obstinate through everything. He did not want to give back\r\nthe money.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What? Was the dispute over money?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes; and a small sum of money too. What did you suppose?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And you travelled over fifty miles for such trifling matters? Wasted\r\nthree days?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They are not trifling matters, Elena Nikolaevna, when my countrymen are\r\ninvolved. It would be wicked to refuse in such cases. I see here that\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t refuse help even to puppies, and I think well of you for it.\r\nAnd as for the time I have lost, that\xe2\x80\x99s no great harm; I will make it up\r\nlater. Our time does not belong to us.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To whom does it belong then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, to all who need us. I have told you all this on the spur of\r\nthe moment, because I value your good opinion. I can fancy how Andrei\r\nPetrovitch must have made you wonder!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You value my good opinion,\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena, in an undertone, \xe2\x80\x98why?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov smiled again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Because you are a good young lady, not an aristocrat... that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA short silence followed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dmitri Nikanorovitch,\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena, \xe2\x80\x98do you know that this is the first\r\ntime you have been so unreserved with me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How\xe2\x80\x99s that? I think I have always said everything I thought to you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, this is the first time, and I am very glad, and I too want to be\r\nopen with you.
