Her thick lips had an insolent curve; her whole\r\nface expressed sensuality, carelessness, and boldness, not without\r\ngoodnature. Himself Shubin had modelled as a lean emaciated rake, with\r\nsunken cheeks, his thin hair hanging in weak wisps about his face, a\r\nmeaningless expression in his dim eyes, and his nose sharp and thin as a\r\ndead man\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nBersenyev turned away with disgust. \xe2\x80\x98A nice pair, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they, my dear\r\nfellow?\xe2\x80\x99 said Shubin; \xe2\x80\x98won\xe2\x80\x99t you graciously compose a suitable title?\r\nFor the first two I have already thought of titles. On the bust shall be\r\ninscribed: \xe2\x80\x9cA hero resolving to liberate his country.\xe2\x80\x9d On the statuette:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook out, sausage-eating Germans!\xe2\x80\x9d And for this work what do you think\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cThe future of the artist Pavel Yakovlitch Shubin?\xe2\x80\x9d Will that do?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Leave off,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98Was it worth while to waste your time\r\non such a ----\xe2\x80\x99 He could not at once fix on a suitable word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Disgusting thing, you mean? No, my dear fellow, excuse me, if anything\r\nought to go to the exhibition, it\xe2\x80\x99s that group.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s simply disgusting,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98And besides, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnonsense. You have absolutely no such degrading tendencies to which,\r\nunhappily, our artists have such a frequent bent. You have simply\r\nlibelled yourself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you think so?\xe2\x80\x99 said Shubin gloomily. \xe2\x80\x98I have none of them, and\r\nif they come upon me, the fault is all one person\xe2\x80\x99s. Do you know,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhe added, tragically knitting his brows, \xe2\x80\x98that I have been trying\r\ndrinking?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nonsense?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, I have, by God,\xe2\x80\x99 rejoined Shubin; and suddenly grinning and\r\nbrightening,--\xe2\x80\x98but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it, my dear boy, the stuff sticks in my\r\nthroat, and my head afterwards is a perfect drum. The great Lushtchihin\r\nhimself--Harlampy Lushtchihin--the greatest drunkard in Moscow, and a\r\nGreat Russian drunkard too, declared there was nothing to be made of me.\r\nIn his words, the bottle does not speak to me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev was just going to knock the group over but Shubin stopped him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99ll do, my dear boy, don\xe2\x80\x99t smash it; it will serve as a lesson, a\r\nscare-crow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If that\xe2\x80\x99s what it is, I will spare your scarecrow then,\xe2\x80\x99 he said. And\r\nnow, \xe2\x80\x98Long live eternal true art!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Long live true art!\xe2\x80\x99 put in Shubin. \xe2\x80\x98By art the good is better and the\r\nbad is not all loss!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe friends shook hands warmly and parted.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXI\r\n\r\n\r\nElena\xe2\x80\x99s first sensation on awakening was one of happy consternation.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it possible? Is it possible?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked herself, and her heart\r\ngrew faint with happiness. Recollections came rushing on her... she was\r\noverwhelmed by them. Then again she was enfolded by the blissful peace\r\nof triumph. But in the course of the morning, Elena gradually became\r\npossessed by a spirit of unrest, and for the remainder of the day she\r\nfelt listless and weary. It was true she knew now what she wanted, but\r\nthat made it no easier for her. That never-to-be forgotten meeting had\r\ncast her for ever out of the old groove; she was no longer at the same\r\nstandpoint, she was far away, and yet everything went on about her\r\nin its accustomed order, everything pursued its own course as though\r\nnothing were changed; the old life moved on its old way, reckoning on\r\nElena\xe2\x80\x99s interest and co-operation as of old. She tried to begin a letter\r\nto Insarov, but that too was a failure; the words came on to paper\r\neither lifeless or false. Her diary she had put an end to by drawing a\r\nthick stroke under the last line. That was the past, and every thought,\r\nall her soul, was turned now to the future. Her heart was heavy. To sit\r\nwith her mother who suspected nothing, to listen to her, answer her and\r\ntalk to her, seemed to Elena something wicked; she felt the presence of\r\na kind of falseness in her, she suffered though she had nothing to blush\r\nfor; more than once an almost irresistible desire sprang up in her\r\nheart to tell everything without reserve, whatever might come of it\r\nafterwards. \xe2\x80\x98Why,\xe2\x80\x99 she thought, \xe2\x80\x98did not Dmitri take me away then, from\r\nthat little chapel, wherever he wanted to go? Didn\xe2\x80\x99t he tell me I was\r\nhis wife before God? What am I here for?\xe2\x80\x99 She suddenly began to feel shy\r\nof every one, even of Uvar Ivanovitch, who was flourishing his fingers\r\nin more perplexity than ever. Now everything about her seemed neither\r\nsweet nor friendly, nor even a dream, but, like a nightmare, lay,\r\nan immovable dead load, on her heart; seeming to reproach her and be\r\nindignant with her, and not to care to know about her....\xe2\x80\x99You are ours\r\nin spite of everything,\xe2\x80\x99 she seemed to hear. Even her poor pets, her\r\nill-used birds and animals looked at her--so at least she fancied--with\r\nsuspicion and hostility. She felt conscience-stricken and ashamed of\r\nher feelings. \xe2\x80\x98This is my home after all,\xe2\x80\x99 she thought, \xe2\x80\x98my family, my\r\ncountry.\xe2\x80\x99... \xe2\x80\x98No, it\xe2\x80\x99s no longer your country, nor your family,\xe2\x80\x99 another\r\nvoice affirmed within her. Terror was overmastering her, and she was\r\nvexed with her own feebleness. The trial was only beginning and she was\r\nlosing patience already... Was this what she had promised?\r\n\r\nShe did not soon gain control of herself. But a week passed and then\r\nanother.... Elena became a little calmer, and grew used to her new\r\nposition. She wrote two little notes to Insarov, and carried them\r\nherself to the post: she could not for anything--through shame and\r\nthrough pride--have brought herself to confide in a maid. She was\r\nalready beginning to expect him in person.... But instead of Insarov,\r\none fine morning Nikolai Artemyevitch made his appearance.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXII\r\n\r\n\r\nNo one in the house of the retired lieutenant of guards, Stahov, had\r\never seen him so sour, and at the same time so self-confident and\r\nimportant as on that day. He walked into the drawing-room in his\r\novercoat and hat, with long deliberate stride, stamping with his heels;\r\nhe approached the looking-glass and took a long look at himself,\r\nshaking his head and biting his lips with imperturbable severity. Anna\r\nVassilyevna met him with obvious agitation and secret delight (she never\r\nmet him otherwise); he did not even take off his hat, nor greet her, and\r\nin silence gave Elena his doe-skin glove to kiss. Anna Vassilyevna began\r\nquestioning him about the progress of his cure; he made her no reply.\r\nUvar Ivanovitch made his appearance; he glanced at him and said, \xe2\x80\x98bah!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nHe usually behaved coldly and haughtily to Uvar Ivanovitch, though\r\nhe acknowledged in him \xe2\x80\x98traces of the true Stahov blood.\xe2\x80\x99 Almost all\r\nRussian families of the nobility are convinced, as is well known, of the\r\nexistence of exceptional hereditary characteristics, peculiar to them\r\nalone; we have more than once heard discussions \xe2\x80\x98among ourselves\xe2\x80\x99 of the\r\nPodsalaskinsky \xe2\x80\x98noses,\xe2\x80\x99 and the \xe2\x80\x98Perepreyevsky\xe2\x80\x99 necks. Zoya came in and\r\nsat down facing Nikolai Artemyevitch. He grunted, sank into an armchair,\r\nasked for coffee, and only then took off his hat. Coffee was brought\r\nhim; he drank a cup, and looking at everybody in turn, he growled\r\nbetween his teeth, \xe2\x80\x98_Sortes, s\xe2\x80\x99il vous pla\xc3\xaet_,\xe2\x80\x99 and turning to his wife\r\nhe added, \xe2\x80\x98_et vous, madame, restez, je vous prie_.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey all left the room, except Anna Vassilyevna. Her head was trembling\r\nwith agitation. The solemnity of Nikolai Artemyevitch\xe2\x80\x99s preparations\r\nimpressed her. She was expecting something extraordinary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is it?\xe2\x80\x99 she cried, directly the door was closed.\r\n\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch flung an indifferent glance at Anna Vassilyevna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing special; what a way you have of assuming the air of a victim at\r\nonce!\xe2\x80\x99 he began, quite needlessly dropping the corners of his mouth at\r\nevery word. \xe2\x80\x98I only want to forewarn you that we shall have a new guest\r\ndining here to-day.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who is it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Kurnatovsky, Yegor Andreyevitch. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know him. The head secretary\r\nin the senate.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He is to dine with us to-day?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And was it only to tell me this that you made every one go away?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch again flung a glance--this time one of irony--at\r\nAnna Vassilyevna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Does that surprise you? Defer your surprise a little.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe ceased speaking. Anna Vassilyevna too was silent for a little time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I could have wished----\xe2\x80\x99 she was beginning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know you have always looked on me as an \xe2\x80\x9cimmoral\xe2\x80\x9d man,\xe2\x80\x99 began Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I!\xe2\x80\x99 muttered Anna Vassilyevna, astounded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And very likely you are right. I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to deny that I have in\r\nfact sometimes given you just grounds for dissatisfaction\xe2\x80\x99 (\xe2\x80\x9cmy greys!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n flashed through Anna Vassilyevna\xe2\x80\x99s head), \xe2\x80\x98though you must\r\nyourself allow, that in the condition, as you are aware, of your\r\nconstitution----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And I make no complaint against you, Nikolai Artemyevitch.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_C\xe2\x80\x99est possible_. In any case, I have no intention of justifying\r\nmyself. Time will justify me. But I regard it as my duty to prove to you\r\nthat I understand my duties, and know how to care for--for the welfare\r\nof the family entrusted--entrusted to me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s the meaning of all this?\xe2\x80\x99 Anna Vassilyevna was thinking.\r\n(She could not guess that the preceding evening at the English club\r\na discussion had arisen in a corner of the smoking-room as to the\r\nincapacity of Russians to make speeches. \xe2\x80\x98Which of us can speak? Mention\r\nany one!\xe2\x80\x99 one of the disputants had exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x98Well, Stahov, for\r\ninstance,\xe2\x80\x99 had answered the other, pointing to Nikolai Artemyevitch, who\r\nstood up on the spot almost squealing with delight.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98For instance,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Nikolai Artemyevitch, \xe2\x80\x98my daughter Elena. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou consider that the time has come for her to take a decisive step\r\nalong the path--to be married, I mean to say. All these intellectual\r\nand philanthropic pursuits are all very well, but only up to a certain\r\npoint, up to a certain age. It\xe2\x80\x99s time for her to drop her mistiness, to\r\nget out of the society of all these artists, scholars, and Montenegrins,\r\nand do like everybody else.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How am I to understand you?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Anna Vassilyevna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, if you will kindly listen,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Nikolai Artemyevitch, still\r\nwith the same dropping of the corners of his lips, \xe2\x80\x98I will tell you\r\nplainly, without beating about the bush. I have made acquaintance, I\r\nhave become intimate with this young man, Mr. Kurnatovsky, in the hope\r\nof having him for a son-in-law. I venture to think that when you see\r\nhim, you will not accuse me of partiality or precipitate judgment.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n(Nikolai Artemyevitch was admiring his own eloquence as he talked.) \xe2\x80\x98Of\r\nexcellent education--educated in the highest legal college--excellent\r\nmanners, thirty-three years old, and upper-secretary, a councillor, and\r\na Stanislas cross on his neck. You, I hope, will do me the justice to\r\nallow that I do not belong to the number of those _p\xc3\xa8res de famille_\r\nwho are mad for position; but you yourself told me that Elena Nikolaevna\r\nlikes practical business men; Yegor Andreyevitch is in the first place\r\na business man; now on the other side, my daughter has a weakness for\r\ngenerous actions; so let me tell you that Yegor Andreyevitch, directly\r\nhe had attained the possibility--you understand me--the possibility\r\nof living without privation on his salary, at once gave up the yearly\r\nincome assigned him by his father, for the benefit of his brothers.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who is his father?\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Anna Vassilyevna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98His father? His father is a man well-known in his own line, of the\r\nhighest moral character, _un vrai sto\xc3\xafcien_, a retired major, I think,\r\noverseer of all the estates of the Count B----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah!\xe2\x80\x99 observed Anna Vassilyevna.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah! why ah?\xe2\x80\x99 interposed Nikolai Artemyevitch. \xe2\x80\x98Can you be infected with\r\nprejudice?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, I said nothing----\xe2\x80\x99 Anna Vassilyevna was beginning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, you said, ah!--However that may be, I have thought it well to\r\nacquaint you with my way of thinking; and I venture to think--I venture\r\nto hope Mr. Kurnatovsky will be received _\xc3\xa0 bras ouverts_. He is no\r\nMontenegrin vagrant.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course; I need only call Vanka the cook and order a few extra\r\ndishes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are aware that I will not enter into that,\xe2\x80\x99 said Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch; and he got up, put on his hat, and whistling (he had heard\r\nsome one say that whistling was only permissible in a country villa and\r\na riding court) went out for a stroll in the garden. Shubin watched him\r\nout of the little window of his lodge, and in silence put out his tongue\r\nat him.\r\n\r\nAt ten minutes to four, a hackney-carriage drove up to the steps of the\r\nStahovs\xe2\x80\x99s villa, and a man, still young, of prepossessing appearance,\r\nsimply and elegantly dressed, stepped out of it and sent up his name.\r\nThis was Yegor Andreyevitch Kurnatovsky.\r\n\r\nThis was what, among other things, Elena wrote next day to Insarov:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Congratulate me, dear Dmitri, I have a suitor. He dined with us\r\nyesterday: papa made his acquaintance at the English club, I fancy, and\r\ninvited him. Of course he did not come yesterday as a suitor. But good\r\nmamma, to whom papa had made known his hopes, whispered in my ear\r\nwhat this guest was. His name is Yegor Andreyevitch Kurnatovsky; he\r\nis upper-secretary to the Senate. I will first describe to you his\r\nappearance. He is of medium height, shorter than you, and a good figure;\r\nhis features are regular, he is close-cropped, and wears large whiskers.\r\nHis eyes are rather small (like yours), brown, and quick; he has a flat\r\nwide mouth; in his eyes and on his lips there is a perpetual sort of\r\nofficial smile; it seems to be always on duty there. He behaves very\r\nsimply and speaks precisely, and everything about him is precise; he\r\nmoves, laughs, and eats as though he were doing a duty. \xe2\x80\x9cHow carefully\r\nshe has studied him!\xe2\x80\x9d you are thinking, perhaps, at this minute. Yes;\r\nso as to be able to describe him to you. And besides, who wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t study\r\nher suitor! There\xe2\x80\x99s something of iron in him--and dull and empty at the\r\nsame time--and honest; they say he is really very honest. You, too,\r\nare made of iron; but not like this man. At dinner he sat next me, and\r\nfacing us sat Shubin. At first the conversation turned on commercial\r\nundertakings; they say he is very clever in business matters, and\r\nwas almost throwing up his government post to take charge of a large\r\nmanufacturing business. Pity he didn\xe2\x80\x99t do it! Then Shubin began to talk\r\nabout the theatre; Mr. Kurnatovsky declared and--I must confess--without\r\nfalse modesty, that he has no ideas about art. That reminded me of\r\nyou--but I thought; no, Dmitri and I are ignorant of art in a very\r\ndifferent way though. This man seemed to mean, \xe2\x80\x9cI know nothing of it,\r\nand it\xe2\x80\x99s quite superfluous, still it may be admitted in a well-ordered\r\nstate.\xe2\x80\x9d He seems, however, to think very little about Petersburg and\r\n_comme il faut_: he once even called himself one of the proletariat.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We are working people,\xe2\x80\x99 he said; I thought if Dmitri had said that, I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have liked it; but he may talk about himself, he may boast if\r\nhe likes. With me he is very attentive; but I kept feeling that a very,\r\nvery condescending superior was talking with me. When he means to praise\r\nany one, he says So-and-so is a man of principle--that\xe2\x80\x99s his\r\nfavourite word. He seems to be self-confident, hardworking, capable of\r\nself-sacrifice (you see, I am impartial), that\xe2\x80\x99s to say, of sacrificing\r\nhis own interest; but he is a great despot. It would be woeful to fall\r\ninto his power! At dinner they began talking about bribes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat in many cases the man who accepts a bribe is\r\nnot to blame; he cannot do otherwise. Still, if he is found out,\r\nhe must be punished without mercy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I cried, \xe2\x80\x9cPunish an innocent man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cYes; for the sake of principle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x9cWhat principle?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Shubin. Kurnatovsky\r\nseemed annoyed or surprised, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat needs no explanation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Papa, who seems to worship him, put in \xe2\x80\x9cof course not\xe2\x80\x9d; and to my\r\nvexation the conversation stopped there. In the evening Bersenyev came\r\nand got into a terrific argument with him. I have never seen our good\r\nAndrei Petrovitch so excited. Mr. Kurnatovsky did not at all deny the\r\nutility of science, universities, and so on, but still I understood\r\nAndrei Petrovitch\xe2\x80\x99s indignation. The man looks at it all as a sort of\r\ngymnastics. Shubin came up to me after dinner, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cThis fellow\r\nhere and some one else (he can never bring himself to utter your name)\r\nare both practical men, but see what a difference; there\xe2\x80\x99s the real\r\nliving ideal given to life; and here there\xe2\x80\x99s not even a feeling of duty,\r\nsimply official honesty and activity without anything inside it.\xe2\x80\x9d Shubin\r\nis clever, and I remembered his words to tell you; but to my mind there\r\nis nothing in common between you. You _have faith_, and he has not; for\r\na man cannot _have faith_ in himself only.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He did not go away till late; but mamma had time to inform me that he\r\nwas pleased with me, and papa is in ecstasies. Did he say, I wonder,\r\nthat I was a woman of principle? I was almost telling mamma that I was\r\nvery sorry, but I had a husband already. Why is it papa dislikes you so?\r\nMamma, we could soon manage to bring round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, my dear one! I have described this gentleman in such detail to\r\ndeaden my heartache. I don\xe2\x80\x99t live without you; I am constantly seeing\r\nyou, hearing you. I look forward to seeing you--only not at our house,\r\nas you intended--fancy how wretched and ill at ease we should be!--but\r\nyou know where I wrote to you--in that wood. Oh, my dear one! How I love\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nThree weeks after Kurnatovsky\xe2\x80\x99s first visit, Anna Vassilyevna, to\r\nElena\xe2\x80\x99s great delight, returned to Moscow, to her large wooden house\r\nnear Prechistenka; a house with columns, white lyres and wreaths over\r\nevery window, with an attic, offices, a palisade, a huge green court, a\r\nwell in the court and a dog\xe2\x80\x99s kennel near the well. Anna Vassilyevna\r\nhad never left her country villa so early, but this year with the first\r\nautumn chills her face swelled; Nikolai Artemyevitch for his part,\r\nhaving finished his cure, began to want his wife; besides, Augustina\r\nChristianovna had gone away on a visit to her cousin in Revel; a family\r\nof foreigners, known as \xe2\x80\x98living statues,\xe2\x80\x99 _des poses plastiques_, had\r\ncome to Moscow, and the description of them in the _Moscow Gazette_ had\r\naroused Anna Vassilyevna\xe2\x80\x99s liveliest curiosity. In short, to stay longer\r\nat the villa seemed inconvenient, and even, in Nikolai Artemyevitch\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwords, incompatible with the fulfilment of his \xe2\x80\x98cherished projects.\xe2\x80\x99 The\r\nlast fortnight seemed very long to Elena. Kurnatovsky came over twice\r\non Sundays; on other days he was busy. He came really to see Elena,\r\nbut talked more to Zoya, who was much pleased with him. \xe2\x80\x98_Das ist ein\r\nMann_!\xe2\x80\x99 she thought to herself, as she looked at his full manly face and\r\nlistened to his self-confident, condescending talk. To her mind, no one\r\nhad such a wonderful voice, no one could pronounce so nicely, \xe2\x80\x98I had\r\nthe hon-our,\xe2\x80\x99 or, \xe2\x80\x98I am most de-lighted.\xe2\x80\x99 Insarov did not come to the\r\nStahovs, but Elena saw him once in secret in a little copse by the\r\nMoskva river, where she arranged to meet him. They hardly had time to\r\nsay more than a few words to each other. Shubin returned to Moscow with\r\nAnna Vassilyevna; Bersenyev, a few days later.\r\n\r\nInsarov was sitting in his room, and for the third time looking through\r\nthe letters brought him from Bulgaria by hand; they were afraid to send\r\nthem by post. He was much disturbed by them. Events were developing\r\nrapidly in the East; the occupation of the Principalities by Russian\r\ntroops had thrown all men\xe2\x80\x99s minds into a ferment; the storm was\r\ngrowing--already could be felt the breath of approaching inevitable war.\r\nThe fire was kindling all round, and no one could foresee how far it\r\nwould go--where it would stop. Old wrongs, long cherished hopes--all\r\nwere astir again. Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s heart throbbed eagerly; his hopes too were\r\nbeing realised. \xe2\x80\x98But is it not too soon, will it not be in vain?\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nthought, tightly clasping his hands. \xe2\x80\x98We are not ready, but so be it! I\r\nmust go.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSomething rustled lightly at the door, it flew quickly open, and into\r\nthe room ran Elena.\r\n\r\nInsarov, all in a tremor, rushed to her, fell on his knees before her,\r\nclasped her waist and pressed it close against his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect me?\xe2\x80\x99 she said, hardly able to draw her breath, she\r\nhad run quickly up the stairs. \xe2\x80\x98Dear one! dear one!--so this is\r\nwhere you live? I\xe2\x80\x99ve quickly found you. The daughter of your landlord\r\nconducted me. We arrived the day before yesterday. I meant to write to\r\nyou, but I thought I had better come myself. I have come for a quarter\r\nof an hour. Get up, shut the door.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe got up, quickly shut the door, returned to her and took her by the\r\nhands. He could not speak; he was choking with delight. She looked with\r\na smile into his eyes... there was such rapture in them... she felt shy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Stay,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, fondly taking her hand away from him, \xe2\x80\x98let me take off\r\nmy hat.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe untied the strings of her hat, flung it down, slipped the cape off\r\nher shoulders, tidied her hair, and sat down on the little old sofa.\r\nInsarov gazed at her, without stirring, like one enchanted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sit down,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, not lifting her eyes to him and motioning him to a\r\nplace beside her.\r\n\r\nInsarov sat down, not on the sofa, but on the floor at her feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come, take off my gloves,\xe2\x80\x99 she said in an uncertain voice. She felt\r\nafraid.\r\n\r\nHe began first to unbutton and then to draw off one glove; he drew it\r\nhalf off and greedily pressed his lips to the slender, soft wrist, which\r\nwas white under it.\r\n\r\nElena shuddered, and would have pushed him back with the other hand; he\r\nbegan kissing the other hand too. Elena drew it away, he threw back his\r\nhead, she looked into his face, bent above him, and their lips touched.\r\n\r\nAn instant passed... she broke away, got up, whispered \xe2\x80\x98No, no,\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nwent quickly up to the writing-table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am mistress here, you know, so you ought not to have any secrets from\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, trying to seem at ease, and standing with her back to\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x98What a lot of papers! what are these letters?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov knitted his brows. \xe2\x80\x98Those letters?\xe2\x80\x99 he said, getting up, \xe2\x80\x98you\r\ncan read them.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena turned them over in her hand. \xe2\x80\x98There are so many of them, and the\r\nwriting is so fine, and I have to go directly... let them be. They\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nnot from a rival, eh?... and they\xe2\x80\x99re not in Russian,\xe2\x80\x99 she added, turning\r\nover the thin sheets.\r\n\r\nInsarov came close to her and fondly touched her waist. She turned\r\nsuddenly to him, smiled brightly at him and leant against his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Those letters are from Bulgaria, Elena; my friends write to me, they\r\nwant me to come.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now? To them?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes... now, while there is still time, while it is still possible to\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAll at once she flung both arms round his neck, \xe2\x80\x98You will take me with\r\nyou, yes?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe pressed her to his heart. \xe2\x80\x98O my sweet girl, O my heroine, how\r\nyou said that! But isn\xe2\x80\x99t it wicked, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it mad for me, a homeless,\r\nsolitary man, to drag you with me... and out there too!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe shut his mouth.... \xe2\x80\x98Sh--or I shall be angry, and never come to see\r\nyou again. Why isn\xe2\x80\x99t it all decided, all settled between us? Am I not\r\nyour wife? Can a wife be parted from her husband?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Wives don\xe2\x80\x99t go into war,\xe2\x80\x99 he said with a half-mournful smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh yes, when they can\xe2\x80\x99t stay behind, and I cannot stay here?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena, my angel!.. but think, I have, perhaps, to leave Moscow in a\r\nfortnight. I can\xe2\x80\x99t think of university lectures, or finishing my work.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What!\xe2\x80\x99 interrupted Elena, \xe2\x80\x98you have to go soon? If you like, I will\r\nstop at once this minute with you for ever, and not go home, shall I?\r\nShall we go at once?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov clasped her in his arms with redoubled warmth. \xe2\x80\x98May God so\r\nreward me then,\xe2\x80\x99 he cried, \xe2\x80\x98if I am doing wrong! From to-day, we are one\r\nfor ever!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Am I to stay?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, my pure girl; no, my treasure. You shall go back home to-day, only\r\nkeep yourself in readiness. This is a matter we can\xe2\x80\x99t manage straight\r\noff; we must plan it out well. We want money, a passport----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have money,\xe2\x80\x99 put in Elena. \xe2\x80\x98Eighty roubles.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s not much,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Insarov; \xe2\x80\x98but everything\xe2\x80\x99s a help.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But I can get more. I will borrow. I will ask mamma.... No, I won\xe2\x80\x99t ask\r\nmamma for any.... But I can sell my watch.... I have earrings, too, and\r\ntwo bracelets... and lace.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Money\xe2\x80\x99s not the chief difficulty, Elena; the passport; your passport,\r\nhow about that?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, how about it? Is a passport absolutely necessary?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Absolutely.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena laughed. \xe2\x80\x98What a queer idea! I remember when I was little... a\r\nmaid of ours ran away. She was caught, and forgiven, and lived with us a\r\nlong while... but still every one used to call her Tatyana, the runaway.\r\nI never thought then that I too might perhaps be a runaway like her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you ashamed?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why? Of course it\xe2\x80\x99s better to go with a passport. But if we can\xe2\x80\x99t----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We will settle all that later, later, wait a little,\xe2\x80\x99 said Insarov.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Let me look about; let me think a little. We will talk over everything\r\ntogether thoroughly. I too have money.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena pushed back the hair that fell over on his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O Dmitri! how glorious it will be for us two to set off together!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 said Insarov, \xe2\x80\x98but there, when we get there----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well?\xe2\x80\x99 put in Elena, \xe2\x80\x98and won\xe2\x80\x99t it be glorious to die together too?\r\nbut no, why should we die? We will live, we are young. How old are you?\r\nTwenty-six?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, twenty-six.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And I am twenty. There is plenty of time before us. Ah, you tried to\r\nrun away from me? You did not want a Russian\xe2\x80\x99s love, you Bulgarian! Let\r\nme see you trying to escape from me now! What would have become of us,\r\nif I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come to you then!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena, you know what forced me to go away.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I know; you were in love, and you were afraid. But surely you must have\r\nsuspected that you were loved?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I swear on my honour, Elena, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe gave him a quick unexpected kiss. \xe2\x80\x98There, I love you for that too.\r\nAnd goodbye.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You can\xe2\x80\x99t stop longer?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Insarov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, dearest. Do you think it\xe2\x80\x99s easy for me to get out alone? The\r\nquarter of an hour was over long ago.\xe2\x80\x99 She put on her cape and hat. \xe2\x80\x98And\r\nyou come to us to-morrow evening. No, the day after to-morrow. We shall\r\nbe constrained and dreary, but we can\xe2\x80\x99t help that; at least we shall see\r\neach other. Good-bye. Let me go.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe embraced her for the last time. \xe2\x80\x98Ah, take care, you have broken my\r\nwatch-chain. Oh, what a clumsy boy! There, never mind. It\xe2\x80\x99s all the\r\nbetter. I will go to Kuznetsky bridge, and leave it to be mended. If\r\nI am asked, I can say I have been to Kuznetsky bridge.\xe2\x80\x99 She held the\r\ndoor-handle. \xe2\x80\x98By-the-way, I forgot to tell you, Monsieur Kurnatovsky\r\nwill certainly make me an offer in a day or two. But the answer I shall\r\nmake him--will be this----\xe2\x80\x99 She put the thumb of her left hand to the\r\ntip of her nose and flourished the other fingers in the air. \xe2\x80\x98Good-bye\r\ntill we see each other again. Now, I know the way... And don\xe2\x80\x99t lose any\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena opened the door a little, listened, turned round to Insarov,\r\nnodded her head, and glided out of the room.\r\n\r\nFor a minute Insarov stood before the closed door, and he too listened.\r\nThe door downstairs into the court slammed. He went up to the sofa, sat\r\ndown, and covered his eyes with his hands. Never before had anything\r\nlike this happened to him. \xe2\x80\x98What have I done to deserve such love?\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nthought. \xe2\x80\x98Is it a dream?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBut the delicate scent of mignonette left by Elena in his poor dark\r\nlittle room told of her visit. And with it, it seemed that the air was\r\nstill full of the notes of a young voice, and the sound of a light young\r\ntread, and the warmth and freshness of a young girlish body.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nInsarov decided to await more positive news, and began to make\r\npreparations for departure. The difficulty was a serious one. For\r\nhim personally there were no obstacles. He had only to ask for a\r\npassport--but how would it be with Elena? To get her a passport in the\r\nlegal way was impossible. Should he marry her secretly, and should they\r\nthen go and present themselves to the parents?... \xe2\x80\x98They would let us go\r\nthen,\xe2\x80\x99 he thought \xe2\x80\x98But if they did not? We would go all the same. But\r\nsuppose they were to make a complaint... if... No, better try to get a\r\npassport somehow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe decided to consult (of course mentioning no names) one of his\r\nacquaintances, an attorney, retired from practice, or perhaps struck\r\noff the rolls, an old and experienced hand at all sorts of clandestine\r\nbusiness. This worthy person did not live near; Insarov was a whole hour\r\nin getting to him in a very sorry droshky, and, to make matters worse,\r\nhe did not find him at home; and on his way back got soaked to the skin\r\nby a sudden downpour of rain. The next morning, in spite of a rather\r\nsevere headache, Insarov set off a second time to call on the retired\r\nattorney. The retired attorney listened to him attentively, taking snuff\r\nfrom a snuff-box decorated with a picture of a full-bosomed nymph, and\r\nglancing stealthily at his visitor with his sly, and also snuff-coloured\r\nlittle eyes; he heard him to the end, and then demanded \xe2\x80\x98greater\r\ndefiniteness in the statement of the facts of the case\xe2\x80\x99; and observing\r\nthat Insarov was unwilling to launch into particulars (it was against\r\nthe grain that he had come to him at all) he confined himself to the\r\nadvice to provide himself above all things with \xe2\x80\x98the needful,\xe2\x80\x99 and asked\r\nhim to come to him again, \xe2\x80\x98when you have,\xe2\x80\x99 he added, sniffing at the\r\nsnuff in the open snuff-box, \xe2\x80\x98augmented your confidence and decreased\r\nyour diffidence\xe2\x80\x99 (he talked with a broad accent). \xe2\x80\x98A passport,\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nadded, as though to himself, \xe2\x80\x98is a thing that can be arranged; you go\r\na journey, for instance; who\xe2\x80\x99s to tell whether you\xe2\x80\x99re Marya Bredihin\r\nor Karolina Vogel-meier?\xe2\x80\x99 A feeling of nausea came over Insarov, but he\r\nthanked the attorney, and promised to come to him again in a day or two.\r\n\r\nThe same evening he went to the Stahovs. Anna Vassilyevna met him\r\ncordially, reproached him a little for having quite forgotten them,\r\nand, finding him pale, inquired especially after his health. Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch did not say a single word to him; he only stared at him\r\nwith elaborately careless curiosity; Shubin treated him coldly; but\r\nElena astounded him. She was expecting him; she had put on for him the\r\nvery dress she wore on the day of their first interview in the chapel;\r\nbut she welcomed him so calmly, and was so polite and carelessly gay,\r\nthat no one looking at her could have believed that this girl\xe2\x80\x99s fate was\r\nalready decided, and that it was only the secret consciousness of happy\r\nlove that gave fire to her features, lightness and charm to all her\r\ngestures. She poured out tea in Zoya\xe2\x80\x99s place, jested, chattered; she\r\nknew Shubin would be watching her, that Insarov was incapable of wearing\r\na mask, and incapable of appearing indifferent, and she had prepared\r\nherself beforehand. She was not mistaken; Shubin never took his eyes off\r\nher, and Insarov was very silent and gloomy the whole evening. Elena was\r\nso happy that she even felt an inclination to tease him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, by the way,\xe2\x80\x99 she said to him suddenly, \xe2\x80\x98is your plan getting on at\r\nall?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov was taken aback.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What plan?\xe2\x80\x99 he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, have you forgotten?\xe2\x80\x99 she rejoined, laughing in his face; he alone\r\ncould tell the meaning of that happy laugh: \xe2\x80\x98Your Bulgarian selections\r\nfor Russian readers?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_Quelle bourde_!\xe2\x80\x99 muttered Nikolai Artemyevitch between his teeth.\r\n\r\nZoya sat down to the piano. Elena gave a just perceptible shrug of the\r\nshoulders, and with her eyes motioned Insarov to the door. Then she\r\ntwice slowly touched the table with her finger, and looked at him. He\r\nunderstood that she was promising to see him in two days, and she gave\r\nhim a quick smile when she saw he understood her. Insarov got up and\r\nbegan to take leave; he felt unwell. Kurnatovsky arrived. Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch jumped up, raised his right hand higher than his head, and\r\nsoftly dropped it into the palm of the chief secretary. Insarov would\r\nhave remained a few minutes longer, to have a look at his rival. Elena\r\nshook her head unseen; the host did not think it necessary to introduce\r\nthem to one another, and Insarov departed, exchanging one last look with\r\nElena. Shubin pondered and pondered, and threw himself into a fierce\r\nargument with Kurnatovsky on a legislative question, about which he had\r\nnot a single idea.\r\n\r\nInsarov did not sleep all night, and in the morning he felt very ill; he\r\nset to work, however, putting his papers into order and writing letters,\r\nbut his head was heavy and confused. At dinner time he began to be in\r\na fever; he could eat nothing. The fever grew rapidly worse towards\r\nevening; he had aching pains in all his limbs, and a terrible headache.\r\nInsarov lay down on the very little sofa on which Elena had lately sat;\r\nhe thought: \xe2\x80\x98It serves me right for going to that old rascal,\xe2\x80\x99 and he\r\ntried to sleep.... But the illness had by now complete mastery of him.\r\nHis veins were throbbing violently, his blood was on fire, his thoughts\r\nwere flying round like birds. He sank into forgetfulness. He lay like a\r\nman felled by a blow on his face, and suddenly, it seemed to him, some\r\none was softly laughing and whispering over him: he opened his eyes with\r\nan effort, the light of the flaring candle smote him like a knife....\r\nWhat was it? the old attorney was before him in an Oriental silk\r\ngown belted with a silk handkerchief, as he had seen him the evening\r\nbefore.... \xe2\x80\x98Karolina Vogelmeier,\xe2\x80\x99 muttered his toothless mouth. Insarov\r\nstared, and the old man grew wide and thick and tall, he was no longer a\r\nman, he was a tree.... Insarov had to climb along its gnarled branches.\r\nHe clung, and fell with his breast on a sharp stone, and Karolina\r\nVogelmeier was sitting on her heels, looking like a pedlar-woman, and\r\nlisping: \xe2\x80\x98Pies, pies, pies for sale\xe2\x80\x99; and there were streams of blood\r\nand swords flashing incessantly.... Elena! And everything vanished in a\r\ncrimson chaos.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXV\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s some one here looks like a locksmith or something of the sort,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nBersenyev was informed the following evening by his servant, who was\r\ndistinguished by a severe deportment and sceptical turn of mind towards\r\nhis master; \xe2\x80\x98he wants to see you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ask him in,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bersenyev.\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x98locksmith\xe2\x80\x99 entered. Bersenyev recognised in him the tailor, the\r\nlandlord of Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do you want?\xe2\x80\x99 he asked him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I came to your honour,\xe2\x80\x99 began the tailor, shifting from one foot to the\r\nother, and at times waving his right hand with his cuff clutched in his\r\nthree last fingers. \xe2\x80\x98Our lodger, seemingly, is very ill.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Insarov?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, our lodger, to be sure; yesterday morning he was still on his\r\nlegs, in the evening he asked for nothing but drink; the missis took\r\nhim some water, and at night he began talking away; we could hear him\r\nthrough the partition-wall; and this morning he lies without a word like\r\na log, and the fever he\xe2\x80\x99s in, Lord have mercy on us! I thought, upon my\r\nword, he\xe2\x80\x99ll die for sure; I ought to send word to the police station, I\r\nthought. For he\xe2\x80\x99s so alone; but the missis said: \xe2\x80\x9cGo to that gentleman,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n she says, \xe2\x80\x9cat whose country place our lodger stayed; maybe he\xe2\x80\x99ll tell\r\nyou what to do, or come himself.\xe2\x80\x9d So I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to your honour, for we\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t, so to say----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev snatched up his cap, thrust a rouble into the tailor\xe2\x80\x99s hand,\r\nand at once set off with him post haste to Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings.\r\n\r\nHe found him lying on the sofa, unconscious and not undressed. His face\r\nwas terribly changed. Bersenyev at once ordered the people of the house\r\nto undress him and put him to bed, while he rushed off himself\r\nand returned with a doctor. The doctor prescribed leeches,\r\nmustard-poultices, and calomel, and ordered him to be bled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is he dangerously ill?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Bersenyev.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, very dangerously,\xe2\x80\x99 answered the doctor. \xe2\x80\x98Severe inflammation\r\nof the lungs; peripneumonia fully developed, and the brain perhaps\r\naffected, but the patient is young. His very strength is something\r\nagainst him now. I was sent for too late; still we will do all that\r\nscience dictates.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe doctor was young himself, and still believed in science.\r\n\r\nBersenyev stayed the night. The people of the house seemed kind, and\r\neven prompt directly there was some one to tell them what was to be\r\ndone. An assistant arrived, and began to carry out the medical measures.\r\n\r\nTowards morning Insarov revived for a few minutes, recognised Bersenyev,\r\nasked: \xe2\x80\x98Am I ill, then?\xe2\x80\x99 looked about him with the vague, listless\r\nbewilderment of a man dangerously ill, and again relapsed into\r\nunconsciousness. Bersenyev went home, changed his clothes, and, taking a\r\nfew books along with him, he returned to Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings. He made up\r\nhis mind to stay there, at least for a time. He shut in Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s bed\r\nwith screens, and arranged a little place for himself by the sofa. The\r\nday passed slowly and drearily. Bersenyev did not leave the room except\r\nto get his dinner. The evening came. He lighted a candle with a shade,\r\nand settled down to a book. Everything was still around. Through the\r\npartition wall could be heard suppressed whispering in the landlord\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom, then a yawn, and a sigh. Some one sneezed, and was scolded in\r\na whisper; behind the screen was heard the patient\xe2\x80\x99s heavy, uneven\r\nbreathing, sometimes broken by a short groan, and the uneasy tossing of\r\nhis head on the pillow.... Strange fancies came over Bersenyev. He found\r\nhimself in the room of a man whose life was hanging on a thread, the man\r\nwhom, as he knew, Elena loved.... He remembered that night when Shubin\r\nhad overtaken him and declared that she loved him, him, Bersenyev! And\r\nnow.... \xe2\x80\x98What am I to do now?\xe2\x80\x99 he asked himself. \xe2\x80\x98Let Elena know of his\r\nillness? Wait a little? This would be worse news for her than what I\r\ntold her once before; strange how fate makes me the go-between between\r\nthem!\xe2\x80\x99 He made up his mind that it was better to wait a little. His eyes\r\nfell on the table covered with heaps of papers... \xe2\x80\x98Will he carry out his\r\ndreams?\xe2\x80\x99 thought Bersenyev. \xe2\x80\x98Can it be that all will come to nothing?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd he was filled with pity for the young life struck down, and he vowed\r\nto himself to save it.\r\n\r\nThe night was an uneasy one. The sick man was very delirious. Several\r\ntimes Bersenyev got up from his little sofa, approached the bed on\r\ntip-toe, and listened with a heavy heart to his disconnected muttering.\r\nOnly once Insarov spoke with sudden distinctness: \xe2\x80\x98I won\xe2\x80\x99t, I won\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\nshe mustn\xe2\x80\x99t....\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev started and looked at Insarov; his face,\r\nsuffering and death-like at the same time, was immovable, and his hands\r\nlay powerless. \xe2\x80\x98I won\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x99 he repeated, scarcely audibly.\r\n\r\nThe doctor came in the morning, shook his head and wrote fresh\r\nprescriptions. \xe2\x80\x98The crisis is a long way off still,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, putting on\r\nhis hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And after the crisis?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Bersenyev.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The crisis may end in two ways, _aut Caesar aut nihil_.\r\n\r\nThe doctor went away. Bersenyev walked a few times up and down the\r\nstreet; he felt in need of fresh air. He went back and took up a book\r\nagain. Raumer he had finished long ago; he was now making a study of\r\nGrote.\r\n\r\nSuddenly the door softly creaked, and the head of the landlord\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndaughter, covered as usual with a heavy kerchief, was cautiously thrust\r\ninto the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here is the lady,\xe2\x80\x99 she whispered, \xe2\x80\x98who gave me a silver piece.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe child\xe2\x80\x99s head vanished quickly, and in its place appeared Elena.\r\n\r\nBersenyev jumped up as if he had been stung; but Elena did not stir, nor\r\ncry out. It seemed as if she understood everything in a single instant.\r\nA terrible pallor overspread her face, she went up to the screen, looked\r\nbehind it, threw up her arms, and seemed turned to stone.\r\n\r\nA moment more and she would have flung herself on Insarov, but Bersenyev\r\nstopped her. \xe2\x80\x98What are you doing?\xe2\x80\x99 he said in a trembling whisper, \xe2\x80\x98you\r\nmight be the death of him!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe was reeling. He led her to the sofa, and made her sit down.\r\n\r\nShe looked into his face, then her eyes ran over him from head to foot,\r\nthen stared at the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Will he die?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked so coldly and quietly that Bersenyev was\r\nfrightened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98For God\xe2\x80\x99s sake, Elena Nikolaevna,\xe2\x80\x99 he began, \xe2\x80\x98what are you saying? He\r\nis ill certainly--and rather seriously--but we will save him; I promise\r\nyou that.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He is unconscious?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked in the same tone of voice as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, he is unconscious at present. That\xe2\x80\x99s always the case at the early\r\nstage of these illnesses, but it means nothing, nothing--I assure you.\r\nDrink some water.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe raised her eyes to his, and he saw she had not heard his answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If he dies,\xe2\x80\x99 she said in the same voice, \xe2\x80\x98I will die too.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAt that instant Insarov uttered a slight moan; she trembled all over,\r\nclutched at her head, then began untying the strings of her hat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What are you doing?\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev asked her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will stay here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You will stay--for long?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, perhaps all day, the night, always--I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98For God\xe2\x80\x99s sake, Elena Nikolaevna, control yourself. I could not of\r\ncourse have any expectation of seeing you here; but still I--assume you\r\nhave come for a short time. Remember they may miss you at home.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They will look for you--find you----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What then?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena Nikolaevna! You see. He cannot now protect you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe dropped her head, seemed lost in thought, raised a handkerchief\r\nto her lips, and convulsive sobs, tearing her by their violence, were\r\nsuddenly wrung from her breast. She threw herself, face downwards, on\r\nthe sofa, trying to stifle them, but still her body heaved and throbbed\r\nlike a captured bird.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena Nikolaevna--for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev was repeating over her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah! What is it?\xe2\x80\x99 suddenly sounded the voice of Insarov.\r\n\r\nElena started up, and Bersenyev felt rooted to the spot. After waiting a\r\nlittle, he went up to the bed. Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s head lay on the pillow helpless\r\nas before; his eyes were closed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is he delirious?\xe2\x80\x99 whispered Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It seems so,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Bersenyev, \xe2\x80\x98but that\xe2\x80\x99s nothing; it\xe2\x80\x99s always so,\r\nespecially if----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When was he taken ill?\xe2\x80\x99 Elena broke in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The day before yesterday; I have been here since yesterday. Rely on\r\nme, Elena Nikolaevna. I will not leave him; everything shall be done. If\r\nnecessary, we will have a consultation.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He will die without me,\xe2\x80\x99 she cried, wringing her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I give you my word I will let you hear every day how his illness goes\r\non, and if there should be immediate danger----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Swear you will send for me at once whenever it may be, day or night,\r\nwrite a note straight to me--I care for nothing now. Do you hear? you\r\npromise you will do that?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I promise before God\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Swear it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I swear.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe suddenly snatched his hand, and before he had time to pull it away,\r\nshe had bent and pressed her lips to it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena Nikolaevna, what are you----\xe2\x80\x99 he stammered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No--no--I won\xe2\x80\x99t have it----\xe2\x80\x99 Insarov muttered indistinctly, and sighed\r\npainfully.\r\n\r\nElena went up to the screen, her handkerchief pressed between her teeth,\r\nand bent a long, long look on the sick man. Silent tears rolled down her\r\ncheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena Nikolaevna,\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev said to her, \xe2\x80\x98he might come to himself\r\nand recognise you; there\xe2\x80\x99s no knowing if that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do harm. Besides,\r\nfrom hour to hour I expect the doctor.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena took her hat from the sofa, put it on and stood still. Her eyes\r\nstrayed mournfully over the room. She seemed to be remembering....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I cannot go away,\xe2\x80\x99 she whispered at last.\r\n\r\nBersenyev pressed her hand: \xe2\x80\x98Try to pull yourself together,\xe2\x80\x99 he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98calm yourself; you are leaving him in my care. I will come to you this\r\nvery evening.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena looked at him, said: \xe2\x80\x98Oh, my good, kind friend!\xe2\x80\x99 broke into sobs\r\nand rushed away.\r\n\r\nBersenyev leaned against the door. A feeling of sorrow and bitterness,\r\nnot without a kind of strange consolation, overcame him. \xe2\x80\x98My good, kind\r\nfriend!\xe2\x80\x99 he thought and shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who is here?\xe2\x80\x99 he heard Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s voice.\r\n\r\nBersenyev went up to him. \xe2\x80\x98I am here, Dmitri Nikanorovitch. How are you?\r\nHow do you feel?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Are you alone?\xe2\x80\x99 asked the sick man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And she?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whom do you mean?\xe2\x80\x99 Bersenyev asked almost in dismay.\r\n\r\nInsarov was silent. \xe2\x80\x98Mignonette,\xe2\x80\x99 he murmured, and his eyes closed\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVI\r\n\r\n\r\nFor eight whole days Insarov lay between life and death. The doctor was\r\nincessantly visiting him, interested as a young man in a difficult case.\r\nShubin heard of Insarov\xe2\x80\x99s critical position, and made inquiries after\r\nhim. His compatriots--Bulgarians--came; among them Bersenyev recognised\r\nthe two strange figures, who had puzzled him by their unexpected visit\r\nto the cottage; they all showed genuine sympathy, some offered to take\r\nBersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s place by the patient\xe2\x80\x99s bed-side; but he would not consent\r\nto that, remembering his promise to Elena. He saw her every day and\r\nsecretly reported to her--sometimes by word of mouth, sometimes in a\r\nbrief note--every detail of the illness. With what sinkings of the heart\r\nshe awaited him, how she listened and questioned him! She was always on\r\nthe point of hastening to Insarov herself; but Bersenyev begged her not\r\nto do this: Insarov was seldom alone. On the first day she knew of his\r\nillness she herself had almost fallen ill; directly she got home,\r\nshe shut herself up in her room; but she was summoned to dinner, and\r\nappeared in the dining-room with such a face that Anna Vassilyevna was\r\nalarmed, and was anxious to put her to bed. Elena succeeded, however, in\r\ncontrolling herself. \xe2\x80\x98If he dies,\xe2\x80\x99 she repeated, \xe2\x80\x98it will be the end\r\nof me too.\xe2\x80\x99 This thought tranquillised her, and enabled her to seem\r\nindifferent. Besides no one troubled her much; Anna Vassilyevna was\r\ntaken up with her swollen face; Shubin was working furiously; Zoya\r\nwas given up to pensiveness, and disposed to read _Werther_; Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch was much displeased at the frequent visits of \xe2\x80\x98the\r\nscholar,\xe2\x80\x99 especially as his \xe2\x80\x98cherished projects\xe2\x80\x99 in regard to\r\nKurnatovsky were making no way; the practical chief secretary was\r\npuzzled and biding his time. Elena did not even thank Bersenyev; there\r\nare services for which thanks are cruel and shameful. Only once at her\r\nfourth interview with him--Insarov had passed a very bad night, the\r\ndoctor had hinted at a consultation--only then she reminded him of his\r\npromise. \xe2\x80\x98Very well, then let us go,\xe2\x80\x99 he said to her. She got up and\r\nwas going to get ready. \xe2\x80\x98No,\xe2\x80\x99 he decided, \xe2\x80\x98let us wait till to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nTowards evening Insarov was rather better.\r\n\r\nFor eight days this torture was prolonged. Elena appeared calm; but she\r\ncould eat nothing, and did not sleep at night. There was a dull ache in\r\nall her limbs; her head seemed full of a sort of dry burning smoke. \xe2\x80\x98Our\r\nyoung lady\xe2\x80\x99s wasting like a candle,\xe2\x80\x99 her maid said of her.\r\n\r\nAt last by the ninth day the crisis was passing over. Elena was sitting\r\nin the drawing-room near Anna Vassilyevna, and, without knowing herself\r\nwhat she was doing, was reading her the _Moscow Gazette_; Bersenyev came\r\nin. Elena glanced at him--how rapid, and fearful, and penetrating, and\r\ntremulous, was the first glance she turned on him every time--and at\r\nonce she guessed that he brought good news. He was smiling; he nodded\r\nslightly to her, she got up to go and meet him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has regained consciousness, he is saved, he will be quite well again\r\nin a week,\xe2\x80\x99 he whispered to her.\r\n\r\nElena had stretched out her arm as though to ward off a blow, and she\r\nsaid nothing, only her lips trembled and a flush of crimson overspread\r\nher whole face. Bersenyev began to talk to Anna Vassilyevna, and Elena\r\nwent off to her own room, dropped on her knees and fell to praying, to\r\nthanking God. Light, shining tears trickled down her cheeks. Suddenly\r\nshe was conscious of intense weariness, laid her head down on the\r\npillow, whispered \xe2\x80\x98poor Andrei Petrovitch!\xe2\x80\x99 and at once fell asleep with\r\nwet cheeks and eyelashes. It was long since she had slept or wept.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nBersenyev\xe2\x80\x99s words turned out only partly true; the danger was over,\r\nbut Insarov gained strength slowly, and the doctor talked of a complete\r\nundermining of the whole system. The patient left his bed for all\r\nthat, and began to walk about the room; Bersenyev went home to his own\r\nlodging, but he came every day to his still feeble friend; and every day\r\nas before he informed Elena of the state of his health. Insarov did\r\nnot dare to write to her, and only indirectly in his conversations with\r\nBersenyev referred to her; but Bersenyev, with assumed carelessness,\r\ntold him about his visits to the Stahovs, trying, however, to give him\r\nto understand that Elena had been deeply distressed, and that now she\r\nwas calmer. Elena too did not write to Insarov; she had a plan in her\r\nhead.\r\n\r\nOne day Bersenyev had just informed her with a cheerful face that the\r\ndoctor had already allowed Insarov to eat a cutlet, and that he would\r\nprobably soon go out; she seemed absorbed, dropped her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Guess, what I want to say to you,\xe2\x80\x99 she said. Bersenyev was confused. He\r\nunderstood her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I suppose,\xe2\x80\x99 he answered, looking away, \xe2\x80\x98you want to say that you wish\r\nto see him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena crimsoned, and scarcely audibly, she breathed, \xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, what then? That, I imagine, you can easily do.\xe2\x80\x99--\xe2\x80\x98Ugh!\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nthought, \xe2\x80\x98what a loathsome feeling there is in my heart!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You mean that I have already before...\xe2\x80\x99 said Elena. \xe2\x80\x98But I am\r\nafraid--now he is, you say, seldom alone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s not difficult to get over,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Bersenyev, still not looking\r\nat her. \xe2\x80\x98I, of course, cannot prepare him; but give me a note. Who\r\ncan hinder your writing to him as a good friend, in whom you take an\r\ninterest? There\xe2\x80\x99s no harm in that. Appoint--I mean, write to him when\r\nyou will come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am ashamed,\xe2\x80\x99 whispered Elena.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Give me the note, I will take it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s no need of that, but I wanted to ask you--don\xe2\x80\x99t be angry with\r\nme, Andrei Petrovitch--don\xe2\x80\x99t go to him to-morrow!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBersenyev bit his lip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah! yes, I understand; very well, very well,\xe2\x80\x99 and, adding two or three\r\nwords more, he quickly took leave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So much the better, so much the better,\xe2\x80\x99 he thought, as he hurried\r\nhome. \xe2\x80\x98I have learnt nothing new, but so much the better. What possessed\r\nme to go hanging on to the edge of another man\xe2\x80\x99s happiness? I regret\r\nnothing; I have done what my conscience told me; but now it is over. Let\r\nthem be! My father was right when he used to say to me: \xe2\x80\x9cYou and I,\r\nmy dear boy, are not Sybarites, we are not aristocrats, we\xe2\x80\x99re not the\r\nspoilt darlings of fortune and nature, we are not even martyrs--we are\r\nworkmen and nothing more. Put on your leather apron, workman, and take\r\nyour place at your workman\xe2\x80\x99s bench, in your dark workshop, and let the\r\nsun shine on other men! Even our dull life has its own pride, its own\r\nhappiness!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe next morning Insarov got a brief note by the post. \xe2\x80\x98Expect me,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nElena wrote to him, \xe2\x80\x98and give orders for no one to see you. A. P. will\r\nnot come.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nInsarov read Elena\xe2\x80\x99s note, and at once began to set his room to rights;\r\nasked his landlady to take away the medicine-glasses, took off his\r\ndressing-gown and put on his coat. His head was swimming and his heart\r\nthrobbing from weakness and delight. His knees were shaking; he dropped\r\non to the sofa, and began to look at his watch. \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s now a quarter to\r\ntwelve,\xe2\x80\x99 he said to himself. \xe2\x80\x98She can never come before twelve: I will\r\nthink of something else for a quarter of an hour, or I shall break down\r\naltogether. Before twelve she cannot possibly come.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe door was opened, and in a light silk gown, all pale, all fresh,\r\nyoung and joyful, Elena came in, and with a faint cry of delight she\r\nfell on his breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are alive, you are mine,\xe2\x80\x99 she repeated, embracing and stroking\r\nhis head. He was almost swooning, breathless at such closeness, such\r\ncaresses, such bliss.\r\n\r\nShe sat down near him, holding him fast, and began to gaze at him with\r\nthat smiling, and caressing, and tender look, only to be seen shining in\r\nthe eyes of a loving woman.\r\n\r\nHer face suddenly clouded over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How thin you have grown, my poor Dmitri,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, passing her hand\r\nover his neck; \xe2\x80\x98what a beard you have.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And you have grown thin, my poor Elena,\xe2\x80\x99 he answered, catching her\r\nfingers with his lips.\r\n\r\nShe shook her curls gaily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s nothing. You shall see how soon we\xe2\x80\x99ll be strong again! The storm\r\nhas blown over, just as it blew over and passed away that day when we\r\nmet in the chapel. Now we are going to live.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe answered her with a smile only.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, what a time we have had, Dmitri, what a cruel time! How can people\r\noutlive those they love? I knew beforehand what Andrei Petrovitch would\r\nsay to me every day, I did really; my life seemed to ebb and flow with\r\nyours. Welcome back, my Dmitri!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe did not know what to say to her. He was longing to throw himself at\r\nher feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Another thing I observed,\xe2\x80\x99 she went on, pushing back his hair--\xe2\x80\x98I made\r\nso many observations all this time in my leisure--when any one is very,\r\nvery miserable, with what stupid attention he follows everything that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing on about him! I really sometimes lost myself in gazing at a fly,\r\nand all the while such chill and terror in my heart! But that\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\npast, all past, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Everything\xe2\x80\x99s bright in the future, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are for me in the future,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Insarov, \xe2\x80\x98so it is bright for\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And for me too! But do you remember, when I was here, not the last\r\ntime--no, not the last time,\xe2\x80\x99 she repeated with an involuntary shudder,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98when we were talking, I spoke of death, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why; I never\r\nsuspected then that it was keeping watch on us. But you are well now,\r\naren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m much better, I\xe2\x80\x99m nearly well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are well, you are not dead. Oh, how happy I am!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA short silence followed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena?\xe2\x80\x99 said Insarov.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, my dearest?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell me, did it never occur to you that this illness was sent us as a\r\npunishment?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena looked seriously at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That idea did come into my head, Dmitri. But I thought: what am I to be\r\npunished for? What duty have I transgressed, against whom have I sinned?\r\nPerhaps my conscience is not like other people\xe2\x80\x99s, but it was silent; or\r\nperhaps I am guilty towards you? I hinder you, I stop you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t stop me, Elena; we will go together.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Dmitri, let us go together; I will follow you.... That is my duty.\r\nI love you.... I know no other duty.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O Elena!\xe2\x80\x99 said Insarov, \xe2\x80\x98what chains every word of yours fastens on\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why talk of chains?\xe2\x80\x99 she interposed. \xe2\x80\x98We are free people, you and I.\r\nYes,\xe2\x80\x99 she went on, looking musingly on the floor, while with one hand\r\nshe still stroked his hair, \xe2\x80\x98I experienced much lately of which I had\r\nnever had any idea! If any one had told me beforehand that I, a young\r\nlady, well brought up, should go out from home alone on all sorts\r\nof made-up excuses, and to go where? to a young man\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings--how\r\nindignant I should have been! And that has all come about, and I feel no\r\nindignation whatever. Really!\xe2\x80\x99 she added, and turned to Insarov.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her with such an expression of adoration, that she softly\r\ndropped her hand from his hair over his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dmitri!\xe2\x80\x99 she began again, \xe2\x80\x98you don\xe2\x80\x99t know of course, I saw you there in\r\nthat dreadful bed, I saw you in the clutches of death, unconscious.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You saw me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe was silent for a little. \xe2\x80\x98And Bersenyev was here?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe nodded.\r\n\r\nInsarov bowed down before her. \xe2\x80\x98O Elena!\xe2\x80\x99 he whispered, \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t dare to\r\nlook at you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why? Andrei Petrovitch is so good. I was not ashamed before him. And\r\nwhat have I to be ashamed of? I am ready to tell all the world that I am\r\nyours.... And Andrei Petrovitch I trust like a brother.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He saved me!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Insarov. \xe2\x80\x98He is the noblest, kindest of men!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes... And do you know I owe everything to him? Do you know that it\r\nwas he who first told me that you loved me? And if I could tell you\r\neverything.... Yes, he is a noble man.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nInsarov looked steadily at Elena. \xe2\x80\x98He is in love with you, isn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nElena dropped her eyes. \xe2\x80\x98He did love me,\xe2\x80\x99 she said in an undertone.\r\n\r\nInsarov pressed her hand warmly. \xe2\x80\x98Oh you Russians,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98you have\r\nhearts of pure gold! And he, he has been waiting on me, he has not slept\r\nat night. And you, you, my angel.... No reproaches, no hesitations...\r\nand all this for me, for me----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, yes, all for you, because they love you. Ah, Dmitri! How strange\r\nit is! I think I have talked to you of it before, but it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\r\nI like to repeat it, and you will like to hear it. When I saw you the\r\nfirst time----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why are there tears in your eyes?\xe2\x80\x99 Insarov interrupted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tears? Are there?\xe2\x80\x99 She wiped her eyes with her handkerchief. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, what\r\na silly boy! He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know yet that people weep from happiness. I\r\nwanted to tell you: when I saw you the first time, I saw nothing special\r\nin you, really. I remember, Shubin struck me much more at first, though\r\nI never loved him, and as for Andrei Petrovitch--oh, there was a moment\r\nwhen I thought: isn\xe2\x80\x99t this _he_? And with you there was nothing of that\r\nsort; but afterwards--afterwards--you took my heart by storm!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have pity on me,\xe2\x80\x99 began Insarov. He tried to get up, but dropped down\r\non to the sofa again at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you?\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Elena anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing.... I am still rather weak. I am not strong enough yet for such\r\nhappiness.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then sit quietly. Don\xe2\x80\x99t dare to move, don\xe2\x80\x99t get excited,\xe2\x80\x99 she added,\r\nthreatening him with her finger. \xe2\x80\x98And why have you left off your\r\ndressing-gown? It\xe2\x80\x99s too soon to begin to be a dandy! Sit down and I will\r\ntell you stories. Listen and be quiet. To talk much is bad for you after\r\nyour illness.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe began to talk to him about Shubin, about Kurnatovsky, and what she\r\nhad been doing for the last fortnight, of how war seemed, judging from\r\nthe newspapers, inevitable, and so directly he was perfectly well again,\r\nhe must, without losing a minute, make arrangements for them to start.\r\nAll this she told him sitting beside him, leaning on his shoulder....\r\n\r\nHe listened to her, listened, turning pale and red. Sometimes he tried\r\nto stop her; suddenly he drew himself up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena,\xe2\x80\x99 he said to her in a strange, hard voice \xe2\x80\x98leave me, go away.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What?\xe2\x80\x99 she replied in bewilderment \xe2\x80\x98You feel ill?\xe2\x80\x99 she added quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No... I\xe2\x80\x99m all right... but, please, leave me now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you. You drive me away?.. What are you doing?\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nsaid suddenly; he had bent over from the sofa almost to the ground,\r\nand was pressing her feet to his lips. \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t do that, Dmitri....\r\nDmitri----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe got up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then leave me! You see, Elena, when I was taken ill, I did not lose\r\nconsciousness at first; I knew I was on the edge of the abyss; even in\r\nthe fever, in delirium I knew, I felt vaguely that it was death coming\r\nto me, I took leave of life, of you, of everything; I gave up hope....\r\nAnd this return to life so suddenly; this light after the darkness,\r\nyou--you--near me, with me--your voice, your breath.... It\xe2\x80\x99s more than\r\nI can stand! I feel I love you passionately, I hear you call yourself\r\nmine, I cannot answer for myself... You must go!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dmitri,\xe2\x80\x99 whispered Elena, and she nestled her head on his shoulder.\r\nOnly now she understood him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena,\xe2\x80\x99 he went on, \xe2\x80\x98I love you, you know that; I am ready to give my\r\nlife for you.... Why have you come to me now, when I am weak, when I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t control myself, when all my blood\xe2\x80\x99s on fire... you are mine, you\r\nsay... you love me----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dmitri,\xe2\x80\x99 she repeated; she flushed all over, and pressed still closer\r\nto him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Elena, have pity on me; go away, I feel as if I should die.... I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand these violent emotions... my whole soul yearns for you ... think,\r\ndeath was almost parting us.. and now you are here, you are in my\r\narms... Elena----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe was trembling all over. \xe2\x80\x98Take me, then,\xe2\x80\x99 she whispered scarcely\r\nabove her breath.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXXIX\r\n\r\n\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch was walking up and down in his study with a scowl\r\non his face. Shubin was sitting at the window with his legs crossed,\r\ntranquilly smoking a cigar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Leave off tramping from corner to corner, please,\xe2\x80\x99 he observed,\r\nknocking the ash off his cigar. \xe2\x80\x98I keep expecting you to speak;\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a rick in my neck from watching you. Besides, there\xe2\x80\x99s something\r\nartificial, melodramatic in your striding.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You can never do anything but joke,\xe2\x80\x99 responded Nikolai Artemyevitch.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You won\xe2\x80\x99t enter into my position, you refuse to realise that I am used\r\nto that woman, that I am attached to her in fact, that her absence is\r\nbound to distress me. Here it\xe2\x80\x99s October, winter is upon us. ... What can\r\nshe be doing in Revel?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She must be knitting stockings--for herself; for herself--not for you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You may laugh, you may laugh; but I tell you I know no woman like her.\r\nSuch honesty; such disinterestedness.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Has she cashed that bill yet?\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Shubin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Such disinterestedness,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Nikolai Artemyevitch; \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nastonishing. They tell me there are a million other women in the world,\r\nbut I say, show me the million; show me the million, I say; _ces femmes,\r\nqu\xe2\x80\x99on me les montre_! And she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t write--that\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s killing me!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re eloquent as Pythagoras,\xe2\x80\x99 remarked Shubin; \xe2\x80\x98but do you know what\r\nI would advise you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When Augustina Christianovna comes back--you take my meaning?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, yes; well, what?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When you see her again--you follow the line of my thought?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, yes, to be sure.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Try beating her; see what that would do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch turned away exasperated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought he was really going to give me some practical advice. But\r\nwhat can one expect from him! An artist, a man of no principles----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No principles! By the way, I\xe2\x80\x99m told your favourite Mr. Kurnatovsky, the\r\nman of principle, cleaned you out of a hundred roubles last night. That\r\nwas hardly delicate, you must own now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What of it? We were playing high. Of course, I might expect--but they\r\nunderstand so little how to appreciate him in this house----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That he thought: get what I can!\xe2\x80\x99 put in Shubin: \xe2\x80\x98whether he\xe2\x80\x99s to be my\r\nfather-in-law or not, is still on the knees of the gods, but a hundred\r\nroubles is worth something to a man who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t take bribes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Father-in-law! How the devil am I his father-in-law? _Vous r\xc3\xaavez, mon\r\ncher_. Of course, any other girl would be delighted with such a suitor.\r\nOnly consider: a man of spirit and intellect, who has gained a position\r\nin the world, served in two provinces----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Led the governor in one of them by the nose,\xe2\x80\x99 remarked Shubin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very likely. To be sure, that\xe2\x80\x99s how it should be. Practical, a business\r\nman----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And a capital hand at cards,\xe2\x80\x99 Shubin remarked again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To be sure, and a capital hand at cards. But Elena Nikolaevna.... Is\r\nthere any understanding her? I should be glad to know if there is any\r\none who would undertake to make out what it is she wants. One day she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncheerful, another she\xe2\x80\x99s dull; all of a sudden she\xe2\x80\x99s so thin there\xe2\x80\x99s no\r\nlooking at her, and then suddenly she\xe2\x80\x99s well again, and all without any\r\napparent reason----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA disagreeable-looking man-servant came in with a cup of coffee, cream\r\nand sugar on a tray.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The father is pleased with a suitor,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Nikolai Artemyevitch,\r\nbreaking off a lump of sugar; \xe2\x80\x98but what is that to the daughter! That\r\nwas all very well in the old patriarchal days, but now we have changed\r\nall that. _Nous avons chang\xc3\xa9 tout \xc3\xa7a_. Nowadays a young girl talks to\r\nany one she thinks fit, reads what she thinks fit; she goes about Moscow\r\nalone without a groom or a maid, just as in Paris; and all that is\r\npermitted. The other day I asked, \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Elena Nikolaevna?\xe2\x80\x9d I\xe2\x80\x99m told\r\nshe has gone out. Where? No one knows. Is that--the proper thing?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Take your coffee, and let the man go,\xe2\x80\x99 said Shubin. \xe2\x80\x98You say yourself\r\nthat one ought not _devant les domestiques_\xe2\x80\x99 he added in an undertone.\r\n\r\nThe servant gave Shubin a dubious look, while Nikolai Artemyevitch took\r\nthe cup of coffee, added some cream, and seized some ten lumps of sugar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was just going to say when the servant came in,\xe2\x80\x99 he began, \xe2\x80\x98that\r\nI count for nothing in this house. That\xe2\x80\x99s the long and short of the\r\nmatter. For nowadays every one judges from appearances; one man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nan empty-headed fool, but gives himself airs of importance, and he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrespected; while another, very likely, has talents which might--which\r\nmight gain him great distinction, but through modesty----\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Aren\xe2\x80\x99t you a born statesman?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Shubin in a jeering voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Give over playing the fool!\xe2\x80\x99 Nikolai Artemyevitch cried with heat. \xe2\x80\x98You\r\nforget yourself! Here you have another proof that I count for nothing in\r\nthis house, nothing!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Anna Vassilyevna ill-uses you... poor fellow!\xe2\x80\x99 said Shubin, stretching.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, Nikolai Artemyevitch, we\xe2\x80\x99re a pair of sinners! You had much\r\nbetter be getting a little present ready for Anna Vassilyevna. It\xe2\x80\x99s her\r\nbirthday in a day or two, and you know how she appreciates the least\r\nattention on your part.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, yes,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Nikolai Artemyevitch hastily. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m much obliged to\r\nyou for reminding me. Of course, of course; to be sure. I have a little\r\nthing, a dressing-case, I bought it the other day at Rosenstrauch\xe2\x80\x99s; but\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know really if it will do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I suppose you bought it for her, the lady at Revel?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, certainly.--I had some idea.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, in that case, it will be sure to do.\xe2\x80\x99 Shubin got up from his\r\nseat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Are we going out this evening, Pavel Yakovlitch, eh?\xe2\x80\x99 Nikolai\r\nArtemyevitch asked with an amicable leer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why yes, you are going to your club.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98After the club... after the club.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShubin stretched himself again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Nikolai Artemyevitch, I want to work to-morrow. Another time.\xe2\x80\x99 And\r\nhe walked off.\r\n\r\nNikolai Artemyevitch scowled, walked twice up and down the room, took a\r\nvelvet box with the dressing-case out of the bureau and looked at it a\r\nlong while, rubbing it with a silk handkerchief.
